Getting Back to Work --- A Post-Vacation Briefing on the State of Play in Energy, Gadgets, Retailing and Other Businesses
Getting Back to Work --- A Post-Vacation Briefing on the State of Play in Energy, Gadgets, Retailing
and Other Businesses
4,206 words
4 September 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Welcome back from summer vacation. It's time to get back to work. What have you been missing? Below,
The Wall Street Journal provides an overview of nine segments of business, ranging from the mining industry
to the tablet wars. These aren't "while you were out" memos, but rather previews of the most important
business issues of the fall and winter ahead.
Autos: Europe's Cloud
Heading into autumn, the global auto industry is facing plenty of uncertainties, including the U.S. economy
and slowing growth in China.
The gravest is Europe. In the final months of the year, the industry is certain to rack up $1 billion or more in
additional losses stemming from Europe. The European operations of General Motors Co. and Ford Motor
Co., Fiat SpA, Renault SA and PSA Peugeot Citroen SA are all squeezed by declining sales and bloated
operations that have proved extremely difficult to downsize. GM, Ford and Peugeot are all deep in the red.
Fiat was profitable in the second quarter, but only because of earnings from majority-owned Chrysler Group
LLC. Renault is operating in the black but profit is falling.
In many ways, the European auto industry seems to be in the midst of a slow-motion collapse. Auto sales
began declining in both Europe and the U.S. after 2007. But the U.S. suffered two horrendous down years
and then began a recovery. This year U.S. auto sales will rise for a third year in a row, and will top 14 million
light vehicles -- just about 12% below their 2007 level.
The year-by-year declines in Europe have been more moderate but have just never stopped. Analysts
estimate auto makers will sell about 12.1 million passenger cars this year in 27 countries that make up
Western and Central Europe. That would be a decline of about 7% from 2011, but more than 20% from the
15.6 million passenger vehicles that were sold in 2007. And with Europe reeling from its financial crisis,
there's no telling when the trend will turn.
Carlos Ghosn, chief executive of Renault, said recently the European industry is headed for "three or four
more years of stagnation."
Powerful unions, labor laws and social norms all make plant closures rare. While 17 U.S. auto plants have
closed since 2007, just three in Europe have, despite the pressure to do so.
Auto makers are making some tentative steps toward downsizing. GM's European unit, which lost $617
million in the first half of 2012, has planned to shorten about 20 work days at two German plants between
now and the end of the year. Peugeot, which had a first-half loss of 819 million euros ($1.03 billion) in the first
half, is closing one French plant and laying off 8,000 workers. Fiat has cut investment in Europe and has also
been cutting work weeks at certain plants.
-- Neal E. Boudette
Energy: The Gas Glut
The summer of 2012 may well be remembered as the moment when the business world accepted that
abundant and inexpensive natural gas wasn't going away anytime soon. It also marked the turning point
when the U.S. energy market began to shake from the consequences.
Coal is hemorrhaging market share to natural gas in power generation. A growing number of companies are
switching from diesel fuel to natural gas to run their vehicles. Meanwhile, the renewable-energy sector is
facing an uphill fight in Congress to extend needed tax credits; subsidizing wind power is a harder case to
make when the U.S. has an alternative in plentiful gas.

--- Page 35 ---
Page 35 of 197 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Energy companies have demonstrated they can wring natural gas -- and oil as well -- from dense rock
formations using a combination of technologies such as hydraulic fracturing. That has triggered a huge
increase in production, and a sharp decline in natural-gas prices.
A growing number of market watchers don't believe the nation is in a gas-supply bubble that will pop. "This is
the way it is. Get used to it," says Rusty Braziel, president of consultant RBN Energy.
The plunge in natural-gas prices has hurt major producers such as Exxon Mobil Corp. and Chesapeake
Energy Corp. And prices aren't expected to go back up anytime soon. Even Hurricane Isaac, which churned
through the heart of the gas-producing region of the Gulf of Mexico, didn't cause a spike, leaving natural-gas
prices well under $3 per million British thermal units.
All this gas has to go somewhere. The market solution has been to burn it to make electricity. This has hurt
coal producers, such as Peabody Energy Corp. and Arch Coal Inc. In April, the power output of gas matched
coal for the first time ever.
Barclays recently noted that there isn't a single analyst predicting that natural-gas supplies will drop far
enough next year to raise prices and let idled coal plants fire up again.
Moreover, new gas pipelines are likely to deliver much more of the fuel to market later this year and into
2013. If these new pipes get filled up, as many expect, the result will be low natural-gas prices well into next
year.
If the glut doesn't end, the only hope for weary energy producers struggling with pinched profits may come
from liquefying more natural gas and exporting it to other countries. This fall, the Department of Energy is
expected to decide how many new export terminals to allow.
"If we want to retain any credibility with free trade," says Severin Borenstein, co-director of the Energy
Institute at the University of California, Berkeley, "we can't come out with an explicit policy against exporting
natural gas."
-- Russell Gold
Technology: Big Fight Over Smaller Tablets
The annual fall gadget brawl is about to swing into gear, with Apple Inc., Amazon.com Inc. and their rivals
readying big product launches in the coming weeks.
This year, the battle has a new starring player: the mini tablet.
After peddling smartphones and tablets, some device makers are expanding their portfolios to a device that is
in-between. The race illustrates just how cutthroat the hardware market has become, with no competitor
willing to give up a potential area of the market.
All eyes, as usual, will be on Apple. The iPhone and iPad maker is expected to start selling a smaller tablet by
the end of the year, according to a person briefed on the matter. (The company also plans to start selling a
new iPhone in September with slightly larger screen, other people have said.)
An earlier salvo is coming from Amazon, which is expected to announce updates to its Kindle Fire tablet this
week. Included in its new line-up: an ad-supported tablet priced lower than similar models, according to
people familiar with the discussions.
Both companies declined to comment.
Google Inc. and Samsung Electronics Co. are trying to stake out their ground in the mini-tablet market, too.
But neither Google's Nexus 7 or the similar Galaxy Tab 2 7.0 from Samsung have so far picked up noticeable
market share from the 9.7-inch iPad, which commands around 70% of the tablet market.
Piper Jaffray predicts the seven-inch tablets will represent 20% of tablet sales in 2012, climbing to 25% in
2014.
Makers of the smaller tablets play up how they are easy to hold in one hand and better for watching shows
and movies than mobile phones. But a bigger draw is lower prices. The Kindle Fire and Google's Nexus 7
start at $199; iPads start at $499.
Expect these entrants to compete not only on price but by highlighting the trove of media -- including
television shows, movies and books -- they offer. Most device makers have sealed a number of media deals
these days, making it hard for one of them to stand out.

--- Page 36 ---
Page 36 of 197 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
There will be plenty of new full-sized tablets hitting the market, too. Microsoft Corp.'s new tablet, the Surface,
debuts this fall. It can be used with a case that has a full keyboard, making it a laptop-tablet hybrid of sorts.
PC makers, such as Dell Inc. and Hewlett-Packard Co. also plan to release laptops that convert into tablets
by detaching or maneuvering the screen.
-- Jessica E. Vascellaro
Industrials: Pinning Hopes on China
U.S. industrial companies like United Technologies Corp., Emerson Electric Co. and Danaher Corp. will be
eyeing China's leadership transition in October for any hint that infrastructure spending is back on Beijing's
agenda. The hope is that China's new leaders will take steps to stimulate growth and thereby juice the sales
of the elevators, medical supplies, air-conditioning compressors and backup power equipment sold by U.S.
industrials.
While China accounts for only about 7% of sales at U.S. industrial companies, according to Credit Suisse
research, it has been a major driver of growth amid sluggish markets at home and in Europe. The slowdown
in China is one of the factors that Wall Street analysts expect will mean lower profits for companies in the
S&P 500 in the third quarter, the first time earnings haven't grown since shortly after the recession ended
about three years ago.
Last year, as the U.S. recovery plodded on, United Technologies was selling thousands of Otis elevators to
housing developments across China. But as China's government moved to damp the housing market earlier
this year, Otis's sales slowed, too.
Companies have been disappointed this year, as China's economic growth fell to a three-year low and factory
activity slowed. Top executives were looking for a pickup in the economy in the second half, but those hopes
have now been pushed into next year.
Provincial and municipal officials across China have been talking about local plans to ratchet up infrastructure
spending, but China analysts caution that any true stimulus spending may not be decided until December,
when senior officials will draw up economic plans for next year.
Meanwhile, U.S. industrial companies also have things to worry about at home. Potential customers are seen
holding back on capital spending as they wait to see how the election turns out and how Congress handles
the threat of $1.2 trillion in automatic spending cuts set to begin in January, a situation referred to as the
"fiscal cliff."
Dave Roberts, chief executive of diversified manufacturer Carlisle Cos., said some potential customers are
holding back on construction of new buildings until after the election. "It doesn't make sense to me, but it has
been said by more than one person," Mr. Roberts saidl.
-- Kate Linebaugh
Retail: Back to School As Holiday Harbinger
Are shoppers back -- or just heading back to school?
Americans spent much of the spring with their wallets and purses firmly shut, despite retailers' efforts to lure
them to the checkout lines. But consumers showed up in full force last month to snap up brightly colored
jeans, oversize "fat boy" backpacks and other back-to-school items. Sales at stores open at least a year rose
6% in August compared with the previous year, two percentage points higher than analysts had expected,
according to a Thomson Reuters index.
But is this sales surge sustainable?
Back-to-school sales are often a good indicator of how a retailer will fare during the end-of-year holidays, but
this year there are reasons for retailers to be cautious. Just days before the sales numbers were released on
Aug. 30, retailers got bad news: The Consumer Confidence Index fell to its lowest level in nine months.
"The way to characterize what is happening with consumers and retailers is fits and starts," said Chris G.
Christopher Jr., senior principal economist at IHS Global Insight. "The August retail sales numbers may be
good, but retailers have to be careful."
Now store executives face a dilemma: If they load up on too much merchandise in coming months but
shoppers pull back, the stores will be left with excess inventory that they will have to discount, which will hurt
profits. But if the stores don't buy enough and shopping stays strong, they will disappoint consumers and lose
potential sales.

--- Page 37 ---
Page 37 of 197 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Right now, retailers are erring on the side of caution. Heading into the back-to-school season, stores
increased their inventory levels at almost half the rate they did in 2011. A year ago, retailers were encouraged
by what appeared to be an economic bounce-back that hurt them when it eventually went slack.
Target Corp. is stocking its shelves conservatively during the second half of the year, but the company will
"chase business when demand accelerates unexpectedly," Target Chief Executive Gregg Steinhafel told
investors recently. As for shoppers, he said, the paradox remains: "We believe our guests will continue to be
both cautious and resilient."
Electronics retailers, which have been battered this year as shoppers turn to online-only merchants like
Amazon.com, are looking to new products to pull consumers in the door. Best Buy Co. and GameStop Corp.
are hoping the arrival in the fall of gadgets with Microsoft's new Windows 8 operating system and Nintendo's
Wii U console will motivate customers to spend during the holidays.
-- Ann Zimmerman
Telecom: Regulatory Mood Hinges on Votes
For the telecom industry, the area code that matters most this fall is 202.
Carriers have plenty to worry about in the coming months, with high-speed networks rolling out and a new
iPhone to sell. But all eyes will be on Washington, where the regulatory approach to big issues like telecom
mergers, spectrum sales and pricing models remains in doubt.
The presidential election in November will determine the political makeup of the Federal Communications
Commission and could set the tone for how the Justice Department reviews mergers.
Under President Obama, the FCC rankled telecom companies by pushing for "net neutrality" rules that would
put new regulations on broadband Internet service; rejecting AT&T Inc.'s attempted $39 billion acquisition of
rival T-Mobile USA; and applying close scrutiny to piecemeal sales of spectrum rights that the industry says
are needed to keep up with rising demand for wireless data services. As a result, relations between the
industry and its regulators are the iciest in years.
A new flashpoint may emerge in the fall. The FCC is expected to start a process of reviewing how much
spectrum any one company should be able to control. Smaller carriers and consumer groups want the FCC to
stop the consolidation of the most valuable, lower-frequency airwaves in the hands of a few companies, while
others such as AT&T want the commission to streamline sales of spectrum rights from one company to
another. The course the review takes could be determined in part by who is running the FCC.
As early as January, the U.S. Court of Appeals in Washington will hear oral arguments in Verizon
Communications Inc.'s challenge to the FCC's new net-neutrality rule that prevents Internet providers from
deliberately blocking or slowing Internet traffic. The rule, Verizon argued, would keep it from testing new
pricing models that would require content sellers like, hypothetically, Netflix Inc. to pick up some of the cost of
transmitting the data. The Republican Party platform, adopted at last week's convention, likens the
net-neutrality rule to "trying to micromanage telecom as if it were a railroad network."
Perhaps the most uncertainty looms around how much industry consolidation the government will allow. The
government's rejection of the T-Mobile deal didn't stop executives, bankers and analysts from exploring other
potential deals, such as a T-Mobile acquisition by its next-largest competitor, Sprint Nextel Corp. Some
executives believe a Mitt Romney administration could have a lighter touch in reviewing mergers and would
take another close look at deal-making if the Republican ticket wins in November.
The money trail suggests the leaders of the country's telecom giants are pulling for Republican victories. In
February, AT&T CEO Randall Stephenson donated $30,800 to the Republican National Committee,
according to the Center for Responsive Politics. In May, Verizon CEO Lowell McAdam cut a $10,000 check to
the National Republican Senatorial Committee. Both men also made several smaller contributions last year to
a handful of Democratic politicians.
-- Anton Troianovski
Publishing: Cheaper E-Books Ahead
A legal decision allowing wider discounting in the digital-book market is likely this fall, with potentially lasting
repercussions for the book industry, most immediately at Barnes & Noble Inc.
U.S. District Judge Denise Cote is expected to approve this month or next a settlement of a civil antitrust
lawsuit negotiated in the spring between the Justice Department and three leading book publishers, say
lawyers who have followed the suit.

--- Page 38 ---
Page 38 of 197 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The settlement clears the way for Amazon.com and other retailers, including Google and Kobo Inc., to sell
new e-books of such top-selling writers as James Patterson and Stephen King for $9.99, or even less,
compared with current prices that range up to $14.99 or more. The e-book edition of the coming J.K. Rowling
novel, "The Casual Vacancy," for example, is $17.99.
In theory, consumers could see sharply discounted e-books within a few weeks of the settlement's approval,
according to one publishing executive. But opponents of the deal are likely to appeal, potentially delaying the
return of discounting for several months, and possibly into 2013.
Much is at stake. When Amazon launched its first Kindle e-reader in November 2007, a major selling point
was the company's $9.99 price for digital best sellers. Although the e-commerce giant often lost money on
each $9.99 sale, the cheap e-book prices helped fuel sales of the device, locking up those buyers as Amazon
customers.
But the market changed when Apple launched its iPad tablet in 2010, striking a deal with five major
publishers that resulted in new pricing arrangements for digital books and sent retail prices higher on many
best sellers. That accord was the target of a civil antitrust lawsuit filed in April by the Justice Department,
which has reached a settlement with three of the publishers under which they agreed to allow discounting for
two years. Two other publishers and Apple haven't settled.
A resumption of discounting would pose challenges for Apple, which sells e-books as a way to drive sales of
its mobile devices, and for book publishers who feel the discounted prices cheapened the value of books. But
the most immediate impact would be felt by Barnes & Noble, which has invested heavily in building its Nook
e-book and e-reader business.
Discounting aside, it isn't clear when Barnes & Noble's digital losses might turn to profits. But if e-book prices
start dropping, and Barnes & Noble has to sell major titles at a loss, its prospects look even more uncertain.
Not only would it have to find a way to finance the losses, lower e-book prices would likely erode sales of
conventional books, an area of continued profits for the chain.
Barnes & Noble and Apple both declined to comment.
In a recent discussion with Wall Street analysts, the company's chief executive, William Lynch, offered only a
modest forecast for the company's Nook business, saying it will reduce "year-to-year losses" in fiscal 2013.
-- Jeffrey A. Trachtenberg
Mining: Party's Over?
The party is winding down for natural-resource companies.
A so-called supercycle, or extended boom, in commodities began about a decade ago and was driven by
rapidly growing demand in China, as well as other emerging countries, for natural resources to accommodate
rising standards of living. But now deceleration in China -- the biggest consumer of resources -- has hit
international markets.
The world's biggest mining company, BHP Billiton Ltd., suspended its ambitious Olympic Dam mine
expansion in Australia, after costs to develop a new, second mine at the site to produce copper and uranium
ballooned to what analysts say could end up as high as $50 billion, many times above their initial estimate of
$5 billion in 2005. Such a high price tag could no longer be justified with demand in China, by far BHP's
biggest market, cooling off.
On Tuesday, Fortescue Metals Group Ltd., the world's fourth-largest iron-ore producer, followed suit, saying it
is slowing an aggressive expansion of mines in Australia by cutting spending and jobs.
Prices for iron ore are down to under $90 a metric ton, more than 30% lower than at the start of this summer,
and could fall further. As prices go, stock-market values are likely to follow. "You typically see the value of the
miner go up faster than the price of the commodity, and it goes down faster, too," said Bruno del Ama, CEO
of Global X Funds, a New York fund operator with a focus on commodities.
Mergers that months ago looked sweet are souring. Glencore International' PLC's proposed $34.8 billion
buyout of Xstrata PLC is on shaky ground. Nationalism remains a concern, with mineral-rich nations wanting
bigger shares in profits and some companies wondering whether such concessions are worth it. Uncertainty
surrounding strife with laborers, tax regimes and cost inflation in certain mineral-rich parts of the world,
notably Africa, is spreading.
Others are feeling the squeeze. Orders are dropping for mining-equipment makers, like Milwaukee-based Joy
Global Inc.

--- Page 39 ---
Page 39 of 197 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The key will be cutting costs as much as possible while continuing to try to guarantee production for when
economic growth resumes.
Mining executives are quick to point out that they are still investing in production. Marius Kloppers, chief
executive of BHP, said in August that BHP was still planning $22 billion in capital expenditures in the next 12
months, up from $20 billion during the last 12 months.
The outlook for some metals is still strong. Copper, for example, is still trading at around $3.30 a pound, only
slightly below its value in the first half of this year.
-- John W. Miller
Health: A Migration To Private Plans
It is a season of uncertainty for much of the health-care industry, but the one thing companies can count on,
industry analysts say, is a massive influx of government-financed patients into private insurance plans.
The industry awaits a presidential election that could yet short-circuit the federal health overhaul, despite
June's U.S. Supreme Court ruling that largely upheld the law. In the meantime, much of the work of
implementing the law is in limbo as states and Washington tune in to the campaigns.
Still, no election outcome could stop Americans from getting older and swelling the enrollment of Medicare
plans, including privately managed Medicare Advantage. States are also scurrying to enroll more patients in
Medicaid managed-care plans because of budget pressures, and the federal health law could balloon their
ranks.
If President Obama wins, the private plans that already oversee nearly half of all Medicaid patients can
expect another 16 million potential customers who would be eligible for the expanded state-federal program
for low-income people.
If Mr. Romney comes out on top, states can expect new leeway to contract with private managed-care plans,
and perhaps changes to Medicare that lean more heavily on the managed-care industry.
"Under reform, you get market expansion, and that's a good thing" for health companies, said Dan Mendelson,
the chief executive of Avalere Health, a consultancy. "Under Romney, it's going to be like managed-care city,"
he said.
Washington and states have been slowly moving to push nine million older and sicker patients covered by
both Medicare and Medicaid into private health plans, a cost-cutting measure that was long taboo because of
Medicare rules protecting members from mandatory managed care. Ohio just awarded $3.5 billion in such
contracts, and more states are expected to follow soon.
A series of major deals played out this summer as insurers sought more members guaranteed by the
government's coffers. Last month, Aetna Inc. bought Medicare and Medicaid-heavy Coventry Health Care for
$5.7 billion. In July, WellPoint Inc. spent $4.46 billion on Amerigroup Corp., one of the largest
Medicaid-focused insurers. Both deals must still clear regulators.
"I'm still betting there will be more consolidation," said Ana Gupte, a Bernstein Research analyst. Acquisition
prices for such companies could rise if Mr. Obama wins the election, assuring new Medicaid gains, she said.
Beyond a more tightly bound public-private health sector, few other forecasts are as certain. Lobbyists for the
insurance industry and employer associations say they are still waiting for important regulations spelling out
details on a range of issues, such as how to manage requirements that they cover people regardless of age
or medical condition. They are losing hope that they will see them ahead of November elections now, they
say.
-- Christopher Weaver and Louise Radnofsky

Dow Tacks On 90, Leading to August Gain --- Third Monthly Increase in Row Follows Bernanke's Pledge on Bolstering the Economy; Gold Reaches...
Friday's Markets
Dow Tacks On 90, Leading to August Gain --- Third Monthly Increase in Row Follows Bernanke's
Pledge on Bolstering the Economy; Gold Reaches Five-Month High
By Jonathan Cheng
853 words
1 September 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The Federal Reserve didn't have to say much to send stocks higher Friday, as investors embraced the
prospect of more central-bank aid for the economy.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average capped a quiet month of steady gains with a rise of 90.13 points, or 0.7%,
on the day to 13090.84. It added 82.16 points, or 0.6%, in August, for the third consecutive monthly advance.
For August, the S&P 500-stock index rose 27.26 points, or 2%, to 1406.58 and the Nasdaq Composite rose
127.44 points, or 4.3%, to 3066.96.
Gold, which has gained on expectations of monetary easing, surged 1.9% Friday to finish at a five-month
high of $1,684.60.
Treasury prices rose, which pushed the yield on the 10-year Treasury note, which moves inversely to price,
down to 1.564%, a nearly four-week low. Many investors expect Treasurys to benefit from any future bond
purchases the Fed could pursue in a bid to stimulate the economy.
All it took was for the Fed to reiterate much of the same language it has adhered to for much of the year,
pledging to stand ready in case the recovery falters. In recent weeks, a gradual improvement in the U.S.
economic indicators had led some to worry that Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke could back off his promise --
spoiling a summer rally that has helped lift the Dow 5.6% over the past three months.
But his remarks, delivered at an annual conference at Jackson Hole, Wyo., assuaged those who had feared
Mr. Bernanke could back away from further aid, given the closeness of the U.S. presidential election, which
some investors argued would damp any desire by the Fed to act.
"It's a repetition of what we already know, but more of the same was enough for today," said Michael
Sansoterra, portfolio manager of the RidgeWorth Large Cap Growth Fund. "They're saying that they're
definitely ready to go if things fall through, but not today -- because it's not perilous today."
At first, the market reaction to Mr. Bernanke's remarks was negative. The Dow fell 70 points in a matter of
minutes after the Fed stopped short of immediately announcing a new bond-buying program, known on Wall
Street as QE3, and highlighted the limits of central-bank policy in turning around the economy.
But many investors largely came around to the view that Mr. Bernanke could have simply been laying the
groundwork for such a move at the next Fed meeting, scheduled for Sept. 12 and 13.
"He left the door open for assistance if needed, and that's really what the market wanted to hear," said Jeff
Mortimer, Boston-based director of investment strategy at BNY Mellon Wealth Management.
At the day's highs, the Dow tacked on 151 points. But choppy trading throughout the day highlighted the
impact of low market participation, with many money managers and traders off ahead of the long Labor Day
weekend.
Just 2.8 billion shares changed hands in New York Stock Exchange composite volume, for the fifth
consecutive day trading volumes came in below three billion shares. Wednesday and Thursday were the two
quietest full days of stock trading so far this year, and August marked the lowest monthly average volume
since May 2007.
In corporate news, Facebook shares fell $1.03, or 5.4%, to $18.06, an all-time low, after Bank of America
shares fell $1.03, or 5.4%, to $18.06, an all-time low, after Merrill Lynch cut its view on the stock's future price
to $23, citing the likelihood for market pressure as shares held by insiders and employees become eligible to
trade. Also, BMO Capital Markets cut its Facebook price target to $15.

--- Page 43 ---
Page 43 of 197 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Splunk climbed $3.90, or 13%, to $34.40. The data-analysis software company reported a
narrower-than-expected quarterly loss on revenue that topped forecasts and gave an upbeat sales outlook for
the current quarter.
Zumiez dropped $3.03, or 9.4%, to $29.19 after the teen-apparel retailer reported August same-store sales
below analysts' expectations and provided a fiscal third-quarter earnings outlook under current forecasts.
Throughout August, a month book ended by major Fed announcements, investors pushed stocks higher, with
a surprising lack of volatility. The Dow had just two days of triple-digit declines, and the gains came as
investors plowed money into riskier stocks in the technology, consumer and financial sectors. The
Nasdaq-100 index, which represents many of the biggest tech stocks, surged 4.9% in August, and remains
on the cusp of a 12-year high.
By contrast, the stock market's four "defensive" sectors that are less sensitive to the economy -- utilities,
telecommunications, health-care and consumer-staple stocks -- were the worst places to be in August.
Utilities alone dropped 4.8%.

Fed Sets Stage for Stimulus --- Central Bank Chief Bernanke Defends Past Measures, Says Weak Labor Market Requires More Help
Fed Sets Stage for Stimulus --- Central Bank Chief Bernanke Defends Past Measures, Says Weak
Labor Market Requires More Help
By Jon Hilsenrath
1,070 words
1 September 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
JACKSON HOLE, Wyo. -- A defiant Ben Bernanke sought to shoot down criticism of the Federal Reserve's
easy-money policies and strengthen the case for new efforts by the central bank to bring down what he
described as gravely high unemployment.
Markets have been on edge for months about whether the Fed will launch another large bond-buying
program. Fed Chairman Bernanke, speaking Friday at the central bank's annual retreat here, offered a
vigorous defense of the Fed's $2.3 trillion in bond purchases since 2008, estimating they helped lead to more
than two million jobs -- and signaled that he is strongly considering another installment.
"Central bank securities purchases have provided meaningful support to the economic recovery," he said
adding later that, "we should not rule out the further use of such policies if economic conditions warrant."
Top Fed officials believe such programs, often called quantitative easing, drive down long-term interest rates,
push up stocks and other asset values and soften the value of the dollar. They thereby boost spending,
investment and exports.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average finished with a gain of 90.13 points, at 13090.84, after rebounding from an
initial pullback. Gold prices, which tend to climb on expectations of monetary easing from the Fed, surged
1.9% to $1,684.60 an ounce -- a five-month high. Oil gained 2% and demand for Treasurys, the expected
target of any Fed move, rose. That sent the yield on the 10-year Treasury, which moves inversely to price,
down to 1.564%, a three-week low.
It is a high-stakes moment for the Fed. The U.S. recovery remains weak and unemployment high more than
three years after the recession ended. Global growth appears to be slowing as China's economy loses steam
and much of Europe falls into recession. The European Central Bank meets Thursday amid speculation it is
contemplating new action to combat financial turmoil in the region.
At home, the presidential election is heating up, with the Republican convention over and Democrats
gathering this coming week. If Mr. Bernanke moves, Republicans could accuse him of meddling in politics. If
he stands pat, Democrats could attack him for standing by at a time of 8.3% unemployment.
The Fed's next policy meeting is Sept. 12 and 13. Other Fed statements in recent weeks have signaled that
some action at the meeting is likely -- either new assurances of low interest rates for a long time, new bond
purchases or a combination of actions. Mr. Bernanke didn't say which course he would choose, but strongly
defended the merits of the tools he might employ.
"No decisions have been made," St. Louis Fed president James Bullard said in an interview at the conference
after Mr. Bernanke's remarks.
Skeptics inside the Fed and out have deep concerns about whether the monetary policies that so far have
failed to deliver strong growth could fuel higher inflation, financial instability or a damaging drop in the value
of the dollar. Mr. Bernanke delivered point-by-point rebuttals of these and other concerns.
However, if this was Mr. Bernanke's closing argument in the case for more easing, it likely left many skeptics
unpersuaded. Wall Street analysts estimate that a $500 billion bond-buying program might help bring the
unemployment rate down by a few tenths of a percentage point at most.
"The Fed is at a point where another round of quantitative easing would be a mistake," said Martin Feldstein,
a Harvard University professor and former economic adviser to President Ronald Reagan.
The benefits of bond buying would be small, Mr. Feldstein said at Jackson Hole. He added that the
impediments to bringing down unemployment are immense and potentially beyond the Fed's tools. More than

--- Page 45 ---
Page 45 of 197 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
five million Americans have been out of work for 27 weeks or more. Integrating the long-term unemployed
back into the workforce will take a long time, Mr. Feldstein said. The Fed risks overstimulating the economy in
an effort to address this persistent problem, he added.
Some market participants have been wondering if a run of moderately better economic data of late, including
a pickup in hiring and consumer spending, has changed the Fed's thinking about the economy. Mr. Bernanke
left little doubt that he remains unsatisfied with the outlook.
He described high unemployment as a "grave concern not only because of the enormous suffering and waste
of human talent it entails, but also because persistently high levels of unemployment will wreak structural
damage on our economy that could last for years."
Importantly, the Fed chairman said the recovery's weakness, to date at least, wasn't the result of structural
economic problems, such as a mismatch between the skills people have and the skills employers are
seeking. If that were the case, the Fed's efforts to keep interest rates very low would do little good.
Rather, Mr. Bernanke said, he sees three factors hamstringing growth: the hobbled housing market; strains in
credit and financial markets, particularly due to the European debt crisis; and uncertainty about future U.S.
government budget policies. He didn't say whether he thought Fed policy would address these problems, but
lower interest rates do tend to boost housing activity and financial markets.
He did acknowledge that the Fed alone can't fix the economy, saying other government policy makers should
act as well. The Fed "cannot achieve by itself what a broader and more balanced set of economic policies
might achieve . . . it cannot neutralize the fiscal and financial risks that the country faces," Mr. Bernanke said.
Mr. Bernanke has said repeatedly that Fed policy decisions turn on an analysis of the costs and benefits of
different actions.
On Friday, he emphasized the benefits and played down the costs of the Fed's actions, particularly its
controversial bond-buying programs. He said the programs have lowered long-term interest rates by 0.8 to
1.2 percentage points. The cheaper funding raised overall economic output by 3% and increased payroll
employment by two million jobs, his staff has estimated.
---

Oil Needs Reserve at Jackson Hole
Heard on the Street
Oil Needs Reserve at Jackson Hole
By Liam Denning
523 words
31 August 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
[Financial Analysis and Commentary]
Putting your foot on the gas and your hand on the parking brake is a good way to go nowhere. Or at least
maybe that is what policy makers hope when it comes to oil prices.
But what if they are wrong?
If Ben Bernanke soft-launches another round of quantitative easing at Jackson Hole, Wyo., on Friday, similar
to what he did in 2010, oil bulls will cheer. The Federal Reserve chairman's earlier efforts have helped stoke
prices in the past.
In the six months after the first round of Treasury purchases was announced in March 2009, Brent crude oil
rose by more than half. In contrast, gold, usually the more straightforward beneficiary of easy monetary
policy, eked out just 11%. Much the same happened with QE2, with Brent outpacing gold handily in the six
months that followed Mr. Bernanke's speech two years ago. Ditto for "Operation Twist" after it was
announced in September 2011.
Mr. Bernanke can't take all the, er, credit though. For example, QE1 was launched after oil already had
plummeted in a way that gold hadn't, and prices also were buoyed by Chinese stimulus in 2009. Mr.
Bernanke's 2010 Jackson Hole speech, meanwhile, came just ahead of the start of the Arab Spring and, in
particular, the severe disruption to oil supply caused by Libya's civil war.
Still, low or negative real interest rates tend to juice hard assets. And with gasoline averaging above $3.70 a
gallon at the pump, any tailwind from monetary policy could hurt the consumers it is nominally meant to
help. Francisco Blanch of BofA Merrill Lynch estimates more QE could boost oil prices by 14%.
But could another policy measure supposedly being considered offset this effect? Talk of a release of oil from
the Strategic Petroleum Reserve, or SPR, has become louder in recent weeks.
Regardless of the justification for a release, though, it might have little effect and could ultimately end up
worsening things. The last release, announced in June 2011, had at best a temporary effect. The collapse in
prices in August of that year had far more to do with fiscal wrangles in Washington and the capitals of Europe
and Standard & Poor's cut to the U.S. credit rating.
Moreover, as energy economist Phil Verleger points out, expectations count. Almost two-thirds of the
industrialized world's oil stocks are controlled by companies, not governments. A release of SPR barrels,
suggesting downward pressure on prices, would encourage companies to reduce inventories in expectation
of restoring them more cheaply in the future.
Given that outside the U.S. oil and gasoline inventories are low, it is likely that an SPR-inspired price drop
would quickly give way to even greater concerns about the level of stocks available and the potential for
prices to rise when they are rebuilt.
That is before even factoring in wild cards like Iranian tensions, the coming winter in the Northern
Hemisphere or QE3 for that matter.

The Gold Standard Goes Mainstream
The Gold Standard Goes Mainstream
By Seth Lipsky
1,189 words
30 August 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A15
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
An under-reported development of this campaign season is the Republican Party's decision this week to send
Gov. Mitt Romney into the presidential race on a platform effectively calling for a new gold commission. The
realization that America's system of fiat money is part of its economic problem is moving from the fringes of
political discussion to the center.
This is a sharp contrast from the last time a gold commission was convened, in 1981, a decade after
President Nixon abandoned the Bretton Woods system and opened the era of a fiat dollar. The 1981
commission recommended against restoring a gold basis to the dollar. But two members -- Congressman
Ron Paul and businessman-scholar Lewis Lehrman -- dissented and outlined the case for gold.
The new platform doesn't use the word "gold," describing the 1981 United States Gold Commission as
looking at a "metallic basis" for the dollar. But the metal was gold, and the new platform calls for a similar
commission to investigate ways "to set a fixed value for the dollar."
What has stayed with me from 1981 -- I covered the commission as a young editorial writer for this
newspaper -- is how momentum for a new gold standard faded amid the successes of the supply-side
revolution. President Reagan pushed through his tax reductions and Federal Reserve Chairman Paul Volcker
maintained tight money. Inflation was defeated. The value of the dollar, which had sunk below 1/800th of an
ounce of gold during President Carter's last year in office, soared.
The 1981 commission was also stacked against a gold-backed dollar from the start. The ruling philosophy
was monetarism -- which, as propounded by Milton Friedman, seeks to keep prices steady by adjusting the
money supply. The commission's executive director was Anna Schwartz, co-author of Friedman's "Monetary
History of the United States," and the Democratic-controlled House held firm to monetarist orthodoxy.
Today things have changed. Both Friedman and Schwartz died as heroes of capitalism and freedom, but
monetarism lacks the sway it once had. Even Friedman before he died seemed to adjust his thinking on using
the quantity of money as a target. Schwartz predicted that monetary instability would be a breeding ground
for a restoration for the role of gold.
In the ferment within today's Republican Party, the gold standard has become almost the centrist position.
On the left would be those who favor a system of discretionary activism in which brilliant technocrats, such as
Ben Bernanke at the Fed, use their judgment in setting interest rates. A bit to their right would be advocates
of a rule, such as John Taylor's rule linking interest rates to various conditions, or one that requires the Fed
to target the price of gold but stops short of defining the dollar in terms of specie.
In the center would be advocates of a classical gold standard, in which a dollar is defined as a fixed amount
of gold. These include, among others, Mr. Lehrman, James Grant of Grant's Interest Rate Observer,
publisher Steve Forbes, economist Judy Shelton, and Sean Fieler of the American Principles Project.
A bit further to the right would be partisans of the Austrian school of economics, including Rep. Paul. He
advocates less for a gold standard than for an idea of Friedrich Hayek, the Nobel laureate who came to favor
what he called the denationalization of money and a system centered on private coinage and currency that
would compete with government-issued money. Further right are purists such as the radical constitutionalist
Edwin Vieira Jr., who would simply price things in weights of gold or silver.
A good bit of overlap exists among the camps, but Congress has come alive to all points on this spectrum.
Rep. Kevin Brady, a Texas Republican who is vice chairman of the Joint Economic Committee, is seeking to
pass the Sound Dollar Act, which would end the Fed's mandate to keep unemployment down, instead having
the central bank focus only on stable prices. Rep. Paul is pressing the Free Competition in Currency Act,
which would end legal tender and put Hayek's ideas into practice.

--- Page 48 ---
Page 48 of 197 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In the Senate, Jim DeMint, Mike Lee and Rand Paul are offering the Sound Money Promotion Act, which
would remove the tax on the appreciation in the value of gold and silver coins that have been declared legal
tender by the federal or a state government. Utah has already made gold and silver coins legal tender in the
state.
Then there is Mr. Romney. In Paul Ryan he chose a running mate who understands the idea of sound
money. In June 2010, as chairman of the House Budget Committee, Mr. Ryan asked Mr. Bernanke what he
made of record-high prices of gold. (The value of the dollar had just slid to below 1/1,200th of an ounce of
gold; it has since plunged to below 1/1,600th of an ounce.)
"I don't fully understand the movements in the gold price," Mr. Bernanke replied. He confessed his belief that
some people were hedging "against the fact that they view many other investments as being risky and hard to
predict at this point." No wonder the eventual House bill to audit the Fed passed with overwhelming bipartisan
support.
This is the context in which Mr. Romney last week moved so pointedly to distance himself from a suggestion
by one of his advisers, Glenn Hubbard, that Mr. Bernanke should be considered for another term. Mr.
Romney made clear that he would be looking for a new Fed chairman, an important signal from a candidate
who has made some mistakes -- such as suggesting that monetary policy should be kept away from
Congress. In fact, it is precisely to Congress that the Constitution (in Article 1, Section 8) grants the power to
coin money and regulate the value thereof.
The New York Sun, the online paper I edit, has warned that a gold commission could prove to be the
graveyard for sound money -- on the principle that if one wants to bury an idea, one need but name a
commission. But it's possible that a well-conceived and well-staffed gold commission could actually sort out
the debate.
It's no small thing that Mr. Romney's platform calls for a gold commission and an audit of the Fed. The last
Republican to run on a platform calling for a dollar "on a fully convertible gold basis" was Dwight Eisenhower,
who cast the promise aside once in office. That's a strategic misstep for Mr. Romney, should he win in
November, to avoid.
---
Mr. Lipsky is editor of the New York Sun. The recipient in April of a grant in the form of a lifetime achievement
award from the Lehrman Institute, he is writing a book on the constitutional dollar, forthcoming from Basic
Books.
(See related letters: "Letters to the Editor: It's Harder to Fiddle a Gold Standard, Which Is a Plus" -- WSJ
Sept. 8, 2012)

Stocks: Finding New Gleam for Gold Miners --- Investors See Value in Beaten-Down Stock Prices, Newfound Corporate Thrift and Generous...
Stocks: Finding New Gleam for Gold Miners --- Investors See Value in Beaten-Down Stock Prices,
Newfound Corporate Thrift and Generous Dividends
By Matt Day
775 words
29 August 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections & Amplifications
Newmont Mining Corp. has almost doubled its dividend payment in the first half of 2012 compared with the
year-earlier period. In some editions Wednesday, an In the Markets article misstated the company's name as
Newmont Gold Corp.
(WSJ Aug. 30, 2012)
Subscribe to WSJ: http://online.wsj.com?mod=djnwires
(END)
Gold-mining stocks are starting to shine again.
Once the most popular way to invest in gold, miners fell out of favor when exchange-traded funds made it
easier buy and trade the precious metal directly.
But battered prices and vows of corporate discipline have given new life to gold miners, which have recently
outpaced the metal itself.
Since Aug. 1, the NYSE Gold Bugs Index, which tracks the shares of 16 mining companies, is up 10%,
compared with a gain of 3.5% in gold futures.
It is a stark change. Between November and the end of July, the index slumped 28%, a drop four times
steeper than gold's 7% decline.
On Tuesday, the NYSE Gold Bugs Index was up 0.1%. Gold futures ended down 0.4%.
Gold currently is trading at $1,666.50 a troy ounce, just shy of Monday's four-month high. In contrast, the
largest gold miners are currently valued at an average of 14.5 times earnings, below the average of 15.9 for
companies in the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index, and much less than gold miners' historical average of
30, according to Citigroup.
At today's prices, the miners' shares suggest gold prices below $1,400 a troy ounce, a 16% discount to
today's price, said Jeffrey Wright, an analyst with Global Hunter Securities.
"A lot of professional investors are very attracted to gold stocks because they are so cheap," says Matthew
Peterson, a portfolio manager with Newgate Capital, a Greenwich, Conn., money manager that oversees
$1.8 billion.
Mr. Peterson says Newgate has increased its holdings of gold-mining companies in its natural-resources
portfolio to 12%, from 2.5% at the beginning of the year.
Gold-mining shares have been hurt by management missteps, such as cost overruns and ill-timed
acquisitions.
Many investors say the high-profile ousters of some executives and public pledges of thrift from others have
gone a long way toward restoring their faith. After Barrick Gold Corp., the largest gold miner by production,
said last week it was in talks to sell its stake in an African subsidiary -- part of an initiative to better spend
cash -- its shares outpaced the gains in the NYSE miners index.

--- Page 50 ---
Page 50 of 197 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Moves like those taken at Barrick will push future profits beyond expectations and boost shares in the
process, says Adrian Day, president of Adrian Day Asset Management, an investment manager in Maryland
with about $170 million under management.
Other investors aren't willing to bet on such a turnaround.
Michael Cuggino, portfolio manager of the $17 billion Permanent Portfolio family of funds, says he plans to
keep the funds' gold investments in physical metal, in order to better track the value of the metal itself.
Mining companies "have to manage their business, pay dividends and operate a budget," Mr. Cuggino says,
which can dilute the effect of a rise in gold prices.
But if prices fall, miners can still improve profitability and boost production, according to a Citigroup analysis,
which predicts a better return for gold miners than for gold ETFs.
In an environment of flat or lower gold prices, investors "prefer to have exposure to gold miners" that are
profitable and, ideally, pay a dividend, Global Hunter's Mr. Wright said.
Some miners have almost doubled their dividends recently, luring investors frustrated by record-low interest
rates. Newmont Gold Corp., which links its dividend to the gold price, almost doubled its total dividend
payment in the first half of 2012 compared with the same period last year. Gold Resource Corp. in March
adopted a policy that gave stockholders the option of taking their dividend in gold bullion.
Ani Markova, portfolio manager of the $600 million AGF Precious Metals Fund, says she was moving into
beaten-down miners from gold bullion. She highlights Osisko Mining Corp., of Montreal, which is ramping up
production at what the company hopes will become Canada's most productive gold mine.
"We think it's a very attractive asset that a year from today will surprise" on its profitability, Ms. Markova says.
"However, the management team needs to deliver."

Convention Journal -- Swan Song: Paul Fans Bid Him Adieu for Now
Convention Journal -- Swan Song: Paul Fans Bid Him Adieu for Now
By Peter Nicholas
891 words
27 August 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A5
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
TAMPA, Fla. -- Ron Paul won't get the presidential nomination this week, but he got a consolation from his
most devoted supporters on Sunday: a counter-convention that was a tribute to his quixotic bid to win the
White House and a marker of his uneasy relationship with the Republican Party.
The "Paulians" chanted "Paul '16" and "President Paul" inside the Sun Dome on the University of South
Florida campus, about a half-hour's drive from the official convention site. Speakers likened him to Thomas
Jefferson. Adulatory videos celebrated the congressman's military service and his work as a physician who
protected "unborn life," as one of his former patients described it.
Fears that the Paul crowd would disrupt the proceedings this week haven't come to fruition, with Mr. Paul's
campaign apparently choosing not to prolong fights over delegate counts.
Still, while the two sides might be from the same party, they're not cut from the same cloth. Taking the stage
hours into the event, Mr. Paul said party leaders had called him and said he could speak for an hour at the
Republican convention and say anything he wanted.
He paused and then added, "Tomorrow night."
The crowd laughed. Because of the weather, convention organizers have canceled proceedings on Monday.
Mr. Paul spoke for more than an hour, hitting all the touchstones of his eclectic brand of conservatism:
criticism of the Federal Reserve, the need to protect gun rights and the dangers of foreign intervention.
He took aim at federal regulation in general: "The solution is to get the government out of our lives and off our
backs and out of our wallets."
He also said his movement is destined to grow. In a reference to the idea of the Republican Party as a big
tent, he said: "We will get into the tent, believe me. Because we will become the tent."
For all the love directed at Mr. Paul, the crowd seemed resigned to actuarial realities: Mr. Paul is now 77, and
if he were to try running again for president in 2016, he would be over 80.
They sounded bitter about the convention that will play out this week in downtown Tampa. Mr. Paul won't
address the gathering -- though a video tribute to him is planned -- and some of his delegates won't be
seated by a Republican Party now controlled by the Romney-Ryan ticket.
In interviews, many of the people attending what might be the final Ron Paul political rally said they would
vote for neither Mitt Romney nor President Obama.
Eric Hogan, 28, of Pennsylvania, who wore a T-shirt that read, "My President is Paul," said rather than vote
for either of the major party candidates, he will "continue to spread truth by being vocal and being active."
"What matters is the fact that the will of the people is not being heard," he said.
Bill Detzner, 59, of Miami, said of Mr. Romney: "I think he's going to start a war with Iran. He could get us into
World War III. He's not going to end the Fed. . . .There's nothing he's for that I can support."
The run-up to the Republican convention proved a mixed bag for Paul supporters. The GOP platform that is
taking shape includes a nod to the issues championed by Mr. Paul. The proposed platform, which will be
voted on later this week by convention delegates, calls for studying a return to the gold standard and auditing
the Federal Reserve.
The Federal Reserve came in for visceral abuse at the Paul rally. Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke got more
boos from the audience than did President Obama. (Also booed were conservative talk show host Rush

--- Page 52 ---
Page 52 of 197 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Limbaugh, former Fed chairman Alan Greenspan and various Fox News correspondents. Fox is owned by
News Corp., publisher of The Wall Street Journal.)
Addressing the crowd, state Sen. Tom Davis (R., S.C.) called Mr. Bernanke a "traitor" and a "dictator,"
touching off a round of foot-stomping and thunderous cheers.
Mr. Paul's biggest applause came when he urged abolishing the central bank. At that, the crowd broke into
the chant, "End the Fed!"
The future looks less rosy for the Paul movement because of a recent party rule change that makes it tougher
for future Republican candidates to accumulate delegates even if they failed to win a state's popular vote -- a
tactic pursued by Mr. Paul's fans this year.
His supporters regularly out-organized their opponents during the state conventions, where delegates were
awarded, often long after primary election night.
Paul-fest was a different sort of political rally, an eclectic mix of high-minded discussion and red-meat attacks
on the Fed.
For all the focus on Mr. Paul, the rally also sought to pass the torch. A video tribute to Mr. Paul's son, U.S.
Sen. Rand Paul of Kentucky, painted him as the heir to the Paul movement. The audience cheered when
Rand Paul took the stage and called for auditing not just the Fed, but also the Pentagon.
At one point, the crowd chanted "Paul 16." -- and this time, they meant the son.

The Dow Jones Industrial Average banked its first triple-digit gain in three weeks on expectations that the
Federal Reserve stands ready to bolster growth.
The Dow added 100.51 points, or 0.8%, to 13157.97 on Friday, rising for the first time during the week. That

almost 9% since early June. The Dow lost ground on the week, however, dropping 117.23 points, or 0.9%, to

Reports of the letter, dated Aug. 22, followed the release earlier this past week of minutes from the most
recent meeting of the Fed's policy-setting committee, which suggested increasing support within the central
bank for more action to boost growth.
This coming week, Wall Street will focus on the economic-policy symposium hosted by the Federal Reserve
Bank of Kansas City in Jackson Hole, Wyo. Mr. Bernanke will speak there on Friday.
In Europe, the Stoxx Europe 600 rose 0.1% Friday. Even so, the benchmark snapped an 11-week winning
streak.
Watson Pharmaceuticals jumped 4.69, or 6%, to 83.32 after its subsidiary said it got final approval from the

Resources, which surged 1.62, or 6%, to 28.80.

said it plans to reduce staff as part of cost-cutting efforts.

Copper Slips but Still Rises 2% in Week
Copper Slips but Still Rises 2% in Week
By Matt Day
484 words
25 August 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
B4
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Copper this week built on its recent gains, rising nearly 2% on expectations of higher demand should China
expand -- and the U.S. resume -- efforts to stimulate economic growth.
The futures fell slightly Friday, backing off the prior day's one-month high as traders cashed out on renewed
concern about Europe amid a summit of Greek and German leaders.
Copper for September delivery, the most actively traded contract, fell 0.9 cent, or 0.3%, Friday to $3.4835 a
pound on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange. Thinly traded August futures fell 0.8
cent, or 0.2%, to $3.488 a pound.
China's central bank injected $43.9 billion into the money market this past week, the most in seven months,
as it seeks to stir economic activity. The country accounts for about 40% of global copper consumption, and
weak readings on industrial production and manufacturing have spurred beliefs Beijing will add more liquidity
to stoke growth.
Investors' expectations of a cash infusion from the Federal Reserve were raised as details from the bank's
latest policy-making meeting showed officials leaning toward stimulus action unless the economy improves.
Some expect similar moves from the European Central Bank.
"Either more growth or more liquidity will drive prices higher," Jason Schenker, president of Prestige
Economics, said in a report. "And at least one of those is likely to increase in the second half of 2012."
Copper is used in a range of products, including automobiles, appliances and heavy-duty power cables,
making prices sensitive to changes in the economic-growth outlook.
Weak physical demand for copper in China has been largely offset by expectations that supply will remain
constrained. Mining companies have tried to ramp up production to take advantage of prices that are high by
historic standards but have been hampered by geologic challenges as the quality of copper ore declined.
Those conflicting forces, and summer vacations in the U.S. and Europe, have left the metal in lackluster,
sideways trading. But it is up 6.2% since futures hit a five-month low in June.
Freeport-McMoRan Copper & Gold Inc. and Rio Tinto PLC both reported lower copper production during the
first half. Officials at the giant Collahuasi mine in Chile, controlled by Anglo American PLC and Xstrata PLC,
have warned output will fall short of last year's pace.
In the first five months of the year, refined copper supply fell short of demand by 21,000 metric tons, the
International Copper Study Group said this week.
Kurt Pfafflin, a senior broker with Daniels Trading in Chicago, said economic growth will have to pick up for
copper to sustain its gains. "Ultimately, governments are going to do what they can" to support growth, he
said.

WEEKEND INVESTOR --- Wealth Manager: Gold: Waiting for the Rally
WEEKEND INVESTOR --- Wealth Manager: Gold: Waiting for the Rally
By Joe Light
768 words
25 August 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
B9
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Is gold staging a comeback?
Big investors, including asset manager Pacific Investment Management Co. and hedge-fund managers
George Soros and John Paulson, have ratcheted up their stakes in the precious metal recently, citing
economic uncertainty and fears of inflation.
For most of the year, gold has been in a funk. On Friday, it closed at $1,669.80 a troy ounce, its highest
closing price since April, but far below its all-time high of $1,888.70 reached last August.
The investors' reasons for increasing their stakes aren't exactly new. Europe still teeters between union and
breakup. The Federal Reserve may pump billions of dollars into the banking system, which could spur
inflation. And the U.S. economy, while growing, remains anemic.
For small investors, gold's lackluster performance this year is a reminder that the metal can't be counted on
to consistently hedge against economic or inflationary risks. Still, in the short term, there is reason to believe
prices are poised to rise, analysts say.
The first is seasonality. Since the 1970s, gold prices have risen twice as quickly between September and
February as they have between March and August, meaning that the precious metal might be nearing a
turning point, says Cyril Castelli, founding partner of Paris-based research firm Rcube.
Gold also has historically performed well when inflation-adjusted, two-year Treasury yields are low. When
such yields fall between minus-6% and minus-0.1%, as they are now, gold prices have risen an average
5.7% over the next three months, according to an Rcube analysis.
Still, at its core, gold is a speculative investment. Unlike other investments, it doesn't generate cash, and that
makes price/earnings ratios and other traditional valuation measures useless. Instead, proponents argue,
gold is a store of value to protect against economic catastrophe and inflation.
Recently, however, that reason for owning the metal hasn't borne out.
"The government is pumping money into the system, and gold's not doing anything," says James Swanson,
chief investment strategist at asset manager MFS Investment Management. "I can't find a persistent pattern
for how gold behaves."
Gold's inflation-hedging properties have been overblown, says Mehmet Dicle, a finance professor at Loyola
University in New Orleans.
According to an analysis by Prof. Dicle, since 2007, there is little evidence that gold prices have protected
against inflation. Between 1999 and 2007, the evidence is even weaker, he says.
"If anybody thinks including gold in a portfolio is an inflationary hedge, they're in for a big surprise," he says.
Gold's reputation as a good hedge against the stock market also might be undeserved. Between August
2002 and August 2009, to be sure, the average 60-day correlation between gold and the Standard & Poor's
500-stock index was about zero, meaning that gold prices moved independently of stocks.
This year, though, the correlation has been about 0.3, meaning that gold has tended to rise when stocks rise.
Recent spates of monetary stimulus by the Federal Reserve and the European Central Bank might be partly
responsible for the rising correlation, says Martin Murenbeeld, chief economist at Dundee Wealth. In the past,
expectations of monetary stimulus have temporarily caused correlations between stocks and gold to rise, he
says.

--- Page 57 ---
Page 57 of 197 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"The driving force now is the constant theme of stimulus. When we do monetary stimulus, all kinds of risky
assets" tend to correlate, Mr. Murenbeeld says.
Rather than own gold directly, investors should consider an allocation to the stocks of gold-mining
companies, Rcube's Mr. Castelli says.
Right now, mining stocks' prices relative to the value of their net assets -- the so-called price/book ratio -- are
at their lowest point since 2008. That will give them room to rise even if gold itself performs poorly, he says.
An easy way to get exposure to hold mining stocks is through an exchange-traded fund, such as Market
Vectors Gold Miners.
Even if investors want to take an outsize position in gold itself, such as through the SPDR Gold Shares ETF,
they shouldn't put more than 10% of their portfolio into it, Mr. Murenbeeld says.
Investors concerned about inflation can turn to Treasury inflation-protected securities, or TIPS -- which are
Treasury bonds whose principal increases as the consumer-price index rises -- or even short-term Treasury
bills, Prof. Dicle says. Another option: I-Savings Bonds, which pay an interest rate that tracks changes in the
CPI.

Election 2012: Campaign Watch
Election 2012: Campaign Watch
528 words

GOP Platform Panel
Urges Gold Standard
The committee drafting the GOP's platform before next week's convention included a proposal to establish a
commission exploring a U.S. return to a gold standard.
Republicans in Tampa, Fla., citing President Ronald Reagan's gold commission established "to consider the
feasibility of a metallic basis for U.S. currency," this week included the creation of "a similar commission to
investigate possible ways to set a fixed value for the dollar," according to language of the proposal provided
by a Republican National Committee spokeswoman. The Reagan commission "advised against such a
move," the proposal noted.
Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke has warned frequently of the perils of the gold standard, in which the dollar is
valued at a fixed price per unit of gold. In the 1800s, the U.S. was prone to booms and busts in part because
the gold standard left no discretion in the banking system over interest rates or the money supply, Mr.
Bernanke said this spring.
GOP presumptive presidential nominee Mitt Romney gave the idea lukewarm support in January when asked
about it.
-- Kristina Peterson
Convention Planners
Watching Isaac's Path
Republican convention planners were in wait-and-see mode Thursday as Tropical Storm Isaac continued
toward the U.S. in the days before Monday's start of the presidential nominating event in Tampa, Fla.
Florida Gov. Rick Scott said Thursday that it was too early to say what might happen. He said Republican
convention officials would make the ultimate decision of whether to postpone or cancel the event, even as
local county authorities would have the power to issue evacuation orders.
"I don't think we'll have any confusion," Mr. Scott said. "With good information, everybody's going to make the
same decisions."
-- Siobhan Hughes
Bill Clinton Is Starring
In Obama Campaign Ad
The Obama campaign is stepping up its use of the man who may be the president's most powerful surrogate
-- Bill Clinton.
The former president appears in a new TV ad vouching for Mr. Obama's stewardship of the economy. The
goal is to transfer positive feelings voters have about the economy under Mr. Clinton to Mr. Obama.
-- Laura Meckler
Ryan Airs Out His Plans
To Avoid 'Sequester'

--- Page 59 ---
Page 59 of 197 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Rep. Paul Ryan Thursday amplified his opposition to looming defense budget cuts, promising that Mitt
Romney as president would immediately work to reverse those cuts if Congress allows them to take effect.
Campaigning as Mr. Romney's running mate, Mr. Ryan pointed to his own plan, passed by the House in May,
that would block the defense cuts and replace them with cuts in food stamps and other domestic programs.
At issue are about $110 billion in spending cuts -- half from defense programs, half from domestic -- set to
take effect Jan. 2 unless Congress acts to block or replace them with other deficit-reduction measures.
The cuts, known as a "sequester," were approved in mid-2011 as part of a deal to raise the federal debt
ceiling. Mr. Ryan was one of the lawmakers who supported that deal.
-- Janet Hook

Currency Trading Euro Parts Ways With Risky Crowd
Currency Trading Euro Parts Ways With Risky Crowd
By Matthew Walter
1,011 words
24 August 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The euro is going it alone.
After spending much of the past few years trading in lock step with stocks and other risky assets, the euro
has been decidedly out of sync lately. On Thursday, the euro rose while stocks and crude oil fell.
In fact, the euro and the stock market have moved in opposite directions for 10 of the past 13 days.
The euro's trading patterns have taken a marked turn since late June, when European leaders at a summit
agreed on an economic-growth pact and a plan to bail out Spain's banks. Then in July, European Central
Bank President Mario Draghi tried to reassure financial markets by saying he would do "whatever it takes" to
save the currency.
Those moves diminished the euro's link with the ups-and-downs of global growth. Investors increasingly
expect the ECB to take action, such as injecting money into the economy through a bond-buying program or
cutting interest rates, that could weaken the currency.
That means the euro is less likely to ride waves of optimism higher along with other risky assets, some
investors say.
"You are in the process of a structural shift of how the euro is going to be treated," said John Netto, president
of M3 Capital, a proprietary trading fund in New York. "The euro was a currency that investors would go to if
they perceived the economic environment was one that was conducive to taking on risk."
Now that has changed, Mr. Netto said he is selling the euro, which he predicts could fall as low as $1.12, and
buying gold.
In late afternoon trading in New York, the euro was 0.3% higher on the day against the dollar, at $1.2564,
compared with $1.2529 late Wednesday in New York.
The greenback declined amid rising expectations that the Federal Reserve will embark on a fresh round of
stimulus. Meanwhile, stocks fell, with the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index sinking 11.41 points, or 0.8%, to
1402.08.
Despite the recent selloff, U.S. stocks are near their highest levels in five years. The Australian dollar, another
popular risk barometer, has been on the rise and commodities are pushing higher. And though the euro has
risen this week, it is still down 3.1% from where it started 2012 and was recently at a two-year low.
Stocks, the euro and other risky assets typically trade on their own fundamentals and often have no
connection. But they tend to move together amid times of high anxiety among global investors. That was the
case during the global financial crisis in 2008, and with the resurgence of Greece's debt problems in May
2011. It was also true in June of this year, when worries about Spain's debt troubles sent markets reeling.
The euro's correlation -- a mathematical measure of how similarly two assets move -- to stocks, other
currencies and other markets has shrunk steadily since late June.
The euro and the S&P 500 had a correlation of 0.20 Thursday, down from 0.81 at the end of June, ending a
five-month stretch where the connection was growing stronger, according to data provider CQG. A reading of
1 means assets always move in the same direction. A reading of negative-1 suggests they always move in
the opposite direction, while 0 indicates they have no correlation at all.
The euro and the Australian dollar, a currency particularly sensitive to the global economic outlook, had a
correlation of minus-0.01 on Thursday, down from 0.87 in late June and an average of 0.7 since the financial
crisis.

--- Page 61 ---
Page 61 of 197 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Still, the euro has diverged from stocks and other currencies before, only to snap back when the region's debt
problems came to a head. Following a relatively quiet summer, policy discussions in the U.S. and Europe are
set to heat up in coming weeks, and could send the euro tumbling along with other asset classes.
Potential flashpoints include the Federal Reserve's annual symposium next week in Jackson Hole, Wyo.,
followed by Spain's report on its beleaguered banking sector in mid-September and an Oct. 8 meeting of
Greece's international creditors. Spanish and Italian borrowing costs remain elevated, a sign that investors
are worried about the health of two of the euro zone's biggest economies.
"These are deep-seated problems that belie easy solutions. I think conditions are going to remain pretty
tricky" for investors, said Alan Ruskin, head of foreign-exchange strategy at Deutsche Bank.
The shift away from using the euro as a "risk on" asset can be seen in the trading strategies at C-View, a
U.K.-based currency fund managing $360 million. When C-View makes bets on an improving global
economic outlook, the firm is increasingly opting for currencies other than the euro, said Paul Chappell,
C-View's chief investment officer.
Mr. Chappell said he now prefers currencies of countries with faster growth than the euro zone, such as the
Korean won and Norwegian krone
"Our enthusiasm is for the currencies where the fundamentals are good, and we can't pretend to think that
the fundamentals are good in Europe," he said. Since Mr. Draghi, the ECB president, pledged on July 26 to
take more action to preserve the euro zone, the krone is up 2.5% and the won is up 1.2%, compared with a
1.4% gain for the euro.
The gap between the euro and other markets could widen if the ECB moves ahead with its plans, investors
say. Mr. Draghi is proposing to print more euros to buy bonds, and the ECB could cut interest rates again in
coming months. The central bank's goal is to stimulate the economy by lowering borrowing costs and
weakening the currency. In the market, that translates to rising stocks and a falling euro.
