Page 93 of 197 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
U.S. News --- THE OUTLOOK: Troubles Abroad Keep Cash Flowing to U.S.
U.S. News --- THE OUTLOOK: Troubles Abroad Keep Cash Flowing to U.S.
By Sudeep Reddy
862 words
13 August 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Fears the U.S. will go over the "fiscal cliff" at the end of the year suggest not much has changed in
Washington since the debt-ceiling battle hit the struggling economy a year ago.
Also unchanged: Investors still appear convinced that the U.S. is the safest of borrowers, despite rising angst
about government debt around the world.
Why is Treasury debt still seen as one of the world's safest assets? The U.S. "effectively got a pass," said
Tom Porcelli, chief U.S. economist at RBC Capital Markets. "If you needed to fly into the safety of a country,
the U.S. was the best of the worst."
How long this will last depends how long it takes the global backdrop to improve.
The U.S., despite losing its coveted triple-A debt rating from Standard & Poor's last August, remains a
magnet for money fleeing the globe's trouble spots. As Europe's debt crisis rages, turmoil in Greece, Spain or
Italy sends investors rushing into the safe arms of the U.S. government.
Markets continue -- for now -- to worry far more about global economic weakness than rising U.S. obligations,
pushing yields lower on Treasury debt. The nation can borrow for a decade for around 1.6%, a near-record
low, as investors gladly hand over their money expecting almost no return, after factoring in inflation.
Despite the U.S.'s less-expensive borrowing, the threat of more downgrades early next year looms if policy
makers bungle their handling of the "fiscal cliff" -- the umbrella term for the tax increases and spending cuts
that will kick in unless Washington acts.
A credit-rating downgrade, in theory, could increase the government's borrowing costs if investors demand
higher compensation for the added risk. That depends on their alternatives. Last year's U.S. downgrade came
alongside trouble in Europe that worried investors even more.
The U.S. isn't alone in experiencing the counterintuitive effect of lower borrowing costs against the threat of
rating downgrades. Some of the euro zone's stronger members, including Germany, the Netherlands and
France, have downgrade warnings hanging over them due to periodic eruptions in Europe's currency union.
Yet their borrowing costs remain around all-time lows. Germany and other relatively strong nations can sell
some debt at negative yields. That means investors are paying the government to keep their money safe.
Around the world, the risks to government debt, which was long considered one of the safest assets, is
forcing a re-evaluation of what counts as true safety.
The International Monetary Fund recently estimated that worries about governments' fiscal health could
remove one-sixth of the world's supply of "safe" government debt by 2016, or about $9 trillion in assets that
would need to be replaced by other investments. And it comes after a host of private-sector assets, such as
pools of debt packaged by banks, fell out of favor as safe investments during the financial crisis.
The supply of safe assets, which also includes gold and higher-quality corporate debt, is shrinking at
precisely the same time that demand is soaring, as banks and investors seek havens amid market turmoil
and regulatory changes.
One implication: Pension funds and other big investors looking for safe investments may have to move their
money around more frequently, rather than leaving it in one place for a long period, creating more market
volatility.
The governments that receive such inflows could see their currencies climb in value, holding back export
sales, as the Swiss and Japanese have learned recently. Investors could shift into more creative investments,

--- Page 94 ---
Page 94 of 197 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
giving rise to new bubbles. Some assets once considered safe -- mortgage-backed securities, anyone? --
prove less so years later.
The tallest-dwarf phenomenon is giving some governments false signals about the attractiveness of their
debt. A continuing rush out of the most-troubled euro-zone nations and into French triple-A debt, for instance,
is giving France's government hope about its own budget moves, easing pressure on the country to institute
economic overhauls.
U.S. debt is seeing similar effects, relieving some pressure on U.S. lawmakers to act with any sense of
urgency, either on the economy or its debt troubles. "The flight to safety created a lot of complacency," said
Adolfo Laurenti, deputy chief economist at Mesirow Financial. "Governments need incentives to act."
Would U.S. borrowing costs keep dropping if another downgrade hits next year?
"I wouldn't take this as a stylized fact of what is likely to happen," said IMF economist Gian Maria
Milesi-Ferretti, who leads the Fund's reviews of the U.S. If the fiscal outlook remains unfavorable, he said,
"sooner or later there will be a response in bond markets."
That won't be soon "because of the very unsettled situation elsewhere and also because of weak growth
prospects and extreme risk aversion," he said.
When there is fire in the neighborhood, sometimes the biggest house can remain standing the longest.

Parents of Fallen Troops Find a Home for Their Grief
Parents of Fallen Troops Find a Home for Their Grief
By Michael M. Phillips
2,056 words
11 August 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
3
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
LONG BEACH, Calif. -- Tacked to the wall by the kitchen table in Apt. 245 is a blurred photo of Manuel
Souza's son, Chris, grinning beside a Jeep in Vietnam. Pinned above it is a smaller snapshot, square and
faded, of the flag-covered coffin that in 1969 carried Chris home.
Nearby, in Apt. 322, Kathleen and Dave Chappell display the camouflage fatigues of their son, Jason, who
was killed by a suicide bomber eight years ago in Iraq. Hanging next to the uniform is a white banner,
bordered in red, with a gold star in the center, for a century the symbol of American mothers who lost sons in
combat.
The neighbors live at American Gold Star Manor, a 348-unit complex slowly returning to its 60-year mission
as the only dedicated retirement home for bereaved parents of the nation's war dead.
In 1968, the sprawling campus housed 725 parents of troops mostly lost in World War II and the Korean War.
Its population plunged after Vietnam, when the nation's wars became short and relatively bloodless.
But with the U.S. now at war for more than a decade, the vanguard of a new generation of Gold Star mothers
and fathers is trickling in, drawn by an inexpensive home where they can cling to memories and mourn for as
long as they want, even create shrines, while surrounded by others who understand.
"This is a place where people won't tell you to get over it," said resident Sandra Aceves, whose son Fernando
Mendez, a Navy medical corpsman serving with the Marines, was killed in Ramadi, Iraq, in 2004.
Ms. Aceves is among 11 Gold Star parents living at the complex: four whose sons died in Vietnam and seven
in Iraq. Mothers here often roam the grounds in white, the traditional color of Gold Star mothers. They
decorate their homes with their sons' photos and Purple Heart medals. The flags handed to them at graveside
are folded into sharp triangles.
"I know there are other Gold Star mothers who will start coming here soon," said Ms. Aceves.
Eleanor Boyd, then president of the American Gold Star Mothers organization, came up with the idea for the
nonprofit retirement home as the U.S. emerged from World War II, which claimed some 400,000 American
lives.
Mrs. Boyd, who lost two sons in the war, had spotted a large parcel of Navy land and barracks-style housing
in a working-class section of Long Beach. She collected $441 in seed money from other mothers.
The project stalled during years of infighting and litigation until the early 1960s, when large numbers of Gold
Star parents finally began moving into the refurbished housing, stocked with fresh bedspreads, curtains,
refrigerators, stoves and TVs. The rent was about $39 a month, including utilities.
"If you ever need a home . . . or a happier one; if you're lonely. . .or just fed up with snow and sniffles . . .
always remember this lovely place is waiting to become your home too," resident Betty Oliver, from
Cleveland, said in an early recruiting letter. She touted bingo, potluck suppers, sewing bees, shuffleboard and
the camaraderie that mothers would find there.
In 1972, there were 602 Gold Star parents in residence: two from World War I, 543 from World War II, 49
from the Korean War and eight from Vietnam.
The complex's managers, however, saw vacancy signs ahead. The facility was proving less popular with
mothers whose children died in the Vietnam War, a conflict that, unlike World War II, eventually divided many
in the U.S.

--- Page 96 ---
Page 96 of 197 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"For the most part, the Gold Star mothers from Vietnam represented a climate change in women," said Holly
Fenelon, author of two books about Gold Star mothers. "They seemed much more independent than the
mothers of earlier conflicts."
In the mid-1970s, the federal government financed a renovation that reduced the number of units by about
two-thirds. The barracks came down and up sprang two- and three-story buildings with 348 units on 25 acres
planted with jacaranda, cherry and Norfolk Island pine trees.
With the federal loan and subsidized rents came rules. Residents had to be below certain income limits and
at least 62 years old.
Between the new requirements and changing demographics, the complex soon found itself in trouble. In
1979, the board of trustees agreed to admit war widows and others "whose loss and history is similar to that
suffered" by Gold Star mothers. Still, by 1987, four out of every 10 apartments stood empty.
To keep the complex open, managers in the 1990s began admitting any low-income senior citizen, even
those with no military affiliation. Vacancies at the bargain-priced apartments -- a one-bedroom these days
rents for an average of $330 a month -- were quickly filled.
Terry Geiling joined the Gold Star Manor board in 2000 and took over as chief executive in 2008. He found
an institution that had all but lost its connection to the war dead. To restore those ties, Mr. Geiling, a
74-year-old former Navy submariner and IBM executive, first froze the 250-person waiting list and began
moving in needy, elderly veterans. Some 75 have moved in over the past year, and he's hunting for more.
Gold Star parents remain his top priority but they have proved harder to find. Some 6,500 Americans have
died in Afghanistan and Iraq, far fewer than in previous major wars.
About 11% of the fallen are from California, more than any state, followed by Texas with 9% and Florida with
5%, according to the Defense Department. Mr. Geiling's market research found there were few Gold Star
mothers in California both poor enough and old enough to qualify for residency. Still, he said, "I am committed
to move us back to our original mission."
It should be an easy sell; the manor offers subsidized housing on a gated, garden-like campus with a
swimming pool, Jacuzzi, Internet cafe, library and community hall where residents play bingo and learn line
dancing. Mr. Geiling welcomed Gold Star Mothers groups from around California to Memorial Day
observances at the complex, complete with a 21-gun salute. The complex is advertised in the American Gold
Star Mothers newsletter.
Most importantly, Mr. Geiling lobbied the Department of Housing and Urban Development to lower the
admission age to 55. HUD turned him down in 2010. But an agency official said that after the federal loan is
repaid in 2015, the current age and income restrictions would no longer apply.
Mr. Geiling found an exception in government rules that allows younger Gold Star parents -- if they're single
-- to live in 10% of the apartments immediately.
The first beneficiary was Ms. Aceves, a 55-year-old mother who moved in a year ago. "The only thing that
has brought me a measure of comfort, the only thing that has brought me a measure of relief from my pain, is
being with these other mothers to whom I don't have to explain that today I have the blues," Ms. Aceves said.
Every day, she prays with her rosary on the green park bench with the bronze plaque that bears her name
and that of her son, Hospital Corpsman 3rd Class Mendez, who was 27 when he died.
Ms. Aceves has become close to Martha Garcia, a 54-year-old former factory worker, whose son, Staff Sgt.
Juan Garcia, was killed by a sniper in Iraq in 2005. Mrs. Garcia and her husband, 64-year-old retired truck
driver Daniel Garcia, keep Army boots and a helmet in a glass-fronted cabinet in their apartment.
"My son told me, 'If one soldier comes, I'm wounded. If two soldiers come, I'm dead,'" recalled Mrs. Garcia.
"Nobody came." Her daughter-in-law, now estranged from the family, was Sgt. Garcia's next of kin, so she,
not Mrs. Garcia, received the knock on the door.
For a year, Mrs. Garcia was in denial, telling everyone, "No, my son is going to come home." She went
through a phase where she'd walk carelessly into traffic. "I'm better now," she said. "I don't want to die
anymore."
Mrs. Garcia and Ms. Aceves work in a garden plot, growing nectarines and lemons, cucumbers and
tomatoes. "We've gone through the same pain," said Mrs. Garcia, who wears a pin bearing her son's photo.

--- Page 97 ---
Page 97 of 197 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Sgt. Garcia's photo also is taped to the wall of Mr. and Mrs. Chappell's apartment, along with pictures of
dozens of other fallen troops -- the sons of neighbors or people they've met or learned of through the Gold
Star network.
"There's just this bond that you don't have to speak," Mrs. Chappell, 58, said of the other parents. "You just
know."
Their den has transformed into a shrine to Army Spc. Chappell, who was manning a checkpoint at a bridge
over the Euphrates River when an insurgent detonated a car bomb and himself in 2004.
One wall aims to give context to his death: An image of Saddam Hussein's statue being toppled in Baghdad;
a ballot from the Iraqi election that followed; a newspaper front page with the headline, "U.S. Kills Bin Laden."
The computer monitor flashes a close-up of Jason's smiling face. A shelf is filled with Teddy bears sent by
strangers. The mothers say that many can sympathize, but only those who have buried a child can
empathize.
"When somebody says, 'Don't you think it's time to get over it?' it really hurts because they don't understand,"
said Mr. Chappell, 60, a former aerospace engineer. "It's not that we're wallowing in it. We're celebrating his
life. We don't want him to be forgotten."
Evelyn Dean, 84, from Pawtucket, R.I., has lived at Gold Star Manor for a decade. She cruises the grounds
in an electric scooter and collects free food at the community center; the Gold Star parents get first pick.
Sometimes she scrubs clean the bench with the plaque she and her son share. "It's not a good spot under
the tree," she said. "The birds poop on it."
Lance Cpl. James Dean was on patrol on April 14, 1969, two weeks short of his 19th birthday. Somebody
stepped on a mine. He was her only child, and she still refers to him as "Little Jimmy." Her estranged
husband, a World War II vet, hanged himself a few years later.
She moved west and worked in a factory buffing airplane parts. She cleaned houses. She baby-sat.
Ms. Dean carries a cracked photo of her son in her wallet, in his dress-blues. Written on the back is the
address of the V.J. McAloon & Son funeral home in Pawtucket, where her son's body was delivered. She
keeps his Honorable Mention certificate from the Good Writers Club. There's a book of photos of Jimmy in
suspenders and shorts; him sitting on Santa's lap.
"It's too bad, I don't have anyone to give them to," she said, fingering album pages filled with scallop-edged
black-and-whites. "He was a good kid," she said. "I figure life is life. You can't get sick over it."
But she often wonders whether there is reason for her to soldier on. "You never get over it," she said.
"There's something in you that's missing."
Nadia McCaffrey will be the latest Gold Star mother to move into the complex. The French-born woman
married an American serviceman who frequented the Paris restaurant where she worked in 1968. They have
since separated.
Their son, Army Sgt. Patrick McCaffrey, 34, was shot eight times by an Iraqi soldier in 2004. She has turned
his house in Tracy, Calif., into a shelter for needy veterans. Now, at age 67, she is moving to Long Beach for
herself.
"Patrick was my life really," she said. "If you meet another Gold Star mother you really don't have to explain
yourself. We understand each other. We support each other. It's obvious. I think I belong there."

Stocks rallied into Friday's close, with the S&P 500 capping its longest streak of gains since late 2010, as
investors shrugged off an unexpected drop in China's trade surplus.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 42.76 points, or 0.3%, to 13207.95, in thin volume. The advance put
the Dow just 71.37 points shy of its post-financial-crisis closing high on May 1.
The S&P 500-stock index added 3.07 points, or 0.2%, to 1405.87, led by the telecom and industrial sectors.
The gain was the index's sixth in a row, its longest such run since another six-day rally that ended Dec. 14,
2010. The closing level was the highest since April 3.
The Nasdaq Composite Index edged up 2.22 points, or 0.1%, to 3020.86. All three benchmarks rose for the
week, with the S&P 500 notching its fifth consecutive weekly rise, the longest string of such advances since
February.
Investors said hopes of stimulus from officials such as European Central Bank President Mario Draghi
bolstered stocks in recent days. Investors also are awaiting the U.S. Federal Reserve's central-banker
symposium at the end of the month and the next meeting of the organization's policy-setting committee,
starting Sept. 12.
"In general, the markets have been just kind of inching forward on the hopes of additional policy
accommodation," said Andrew Goldberg, global market strategist at J.P. Morgan Chase's mutual funds unit,
which manages $348 billion. "If I'm bearish or pessimistic, do I have much confidence to go out and . . . sell
against a tape that's been moving higher all week? No."
Stocks had traded lower for most of Friday, after China's July trade surplus fell to $25.1 billion from $31.7
billion a month earlier, bucking economists' projections for a rise. Export and import growth both slowed
sharply, indicating continued deceleration in the world's second-largest economy.
"The global situation is slowing, maybe a little more than we thought, and it's really impacting China," said
Paul Zemsky, chief investment officer of multiasset strategies at ING Investment Management U.S., which
manages $170 billion of assets.
In the U.S., import prices for July slipped 0.6% from a month earlier, the Labor Department reported Friday.
Economists expected it to rise 0.1%.
The U.S. budget deficit narrowed during the first 10 months of the government's fiscal year due to higher tax
collections, the Treasury Department reported.
European markets were broadly lower, with the Stoxx Europe 600 down 0.1%, halting a five-day streak of
gains.
Asian markets also fell, with China's Shanghai Composite slipping 0.2% and Japan's Nikkei Stock Average
shedding 1%.
Crude-oil futures slumped 0.5% to $92.87 a barrel, as imports of the commodity by China, the world's
second-largest oil consumer, fell in July to the lowest level in nine months. In addition, the International
Energy Agency cut its oil-demand growth forecast for 2013.
Gold futures added 0.2% to settle at $1,619.70 an ounce. The dollar gained ground against the euro but
slipped against the yen.

India's Wilting Crops Tarnish Gold --- A Dry Spell Means Farmers Can't Afford Jewelry for Weddings; 'Where Is the Money for That?'
Commodities Report
India's Wilting Crops Tarnish Gold --- A Dry Spell Means Farmers Can't Afford Jewelry for Weddings;
'Where Is the Money for That?'
By Tatyana Shumsky in New York and Biman Mukherji in New Delhi
979 words
7 August 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections & Amplifications India is the largest net importer of gold. Articles about gold prices on the In the
Markets page on Aug. 7 and Nov. 16, 2012, incorrectly called it the largest importer. Switzerland imported
more gold than India last year, though much of it was re-exported. By contrast, most of the gold imported by
India stayed in that country. (WSJ May 2, 2013)
A sputtering start to this year's monsoon rains has forced farmers in India to put their gold-buying plans on
hold.
With crops wilting without the heavy seasonal rains that usually start in June, some farmers say it is unlikely
they can afford the gold jewelry that is the centerpiece of most Indian weddings.
As the world's biggest consumer and importer of gold, India helps set the direction of gold prices. The root of
much of the demand comes from India's countryside, home to hundreds of millions of farmers who often bear
the burden of adorning their daughters, nieces and sisters with solid gold necklaces, bracelets and earrings.
The Bombay Bullion Association, India's main gold industry group, estimates that the volume of the country's
second-quarter gold imports slid 60%, to 128 tons, from the year-earlier period, due largely to the weak
monsoon so far. Before the start of rainy season, the group forecast a roughly 20% drop in gold imports for
2012, due to a weaker rupee, which makes gold more expensive for buyers in India, and widespread
closings of jewelry stores earlier this year to protest new taxes. Now, it is projecting a drop closer to 40%.
Gold investors usually keep a close eye on the weather in India, and many are wagering that the weak rainy
season will further undermine gold prices that are down almost 10% from the 2012 highs hit in late February.
Gold futures on Monday finished 0.4% higher at $1,612.90 an ounce, bouncing back from a loss of 0.7% last
week.
Azhar Sheikh Azhar, a 32-year-old farmer with an eight-acre property in Dahegaon Village, India, canceled
plans for a lavish wedding with gifts of gold jewelry for his niece this year.
"Where is the money for that when we are having trouble thinking of the next meal for the family?" Mr. Azhar
said.
Michael Shaoul, chairman of Marketfield Asset Management LLC, a mutual-fund adviser with more than $2.5
billion under management, has recently bet on lower gold prices as the outlook for India's harvests
worsened.
"In India, the monsoon is another negative factor" in addition to high interest rates, which encourages
investors to keep cash in relatively high-yielding savings accounts, Mr. Shaoul said.
Adam Sarhan, founder and chief executive at asset-management firm Sarhan Capital, New York, cited the
poor weather in India as a reason for recently dumping gold after holding the precious metal in his portfolio
for more than a decade.
"As long as [Indian] demand continues to wane, we're going to continue to see downward pressure on the
price of gold," Mr. Sarhan said.
To be sure, other factors could quickly overwhelm the gold market and send prices rising. Decisive moves by
the world's central banks to print money in order to stimulate the economy is likely to send gold surging, said
James Dailey, lead portfolio manager at TEAM Asset Strategy Fund. Any easing could weaken the dollar or
euro -- or both -- and send investors looking for a hedge into gold, he said.

--- Page 101 ---
Page 101 of 197 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"The marginal demand from investors in the developed world would overwhelm anything that is going on
domestically in India," he said.
Moreover, some investors point out that India's dominant role in the gold market has been fading in recent
years as its imports of the precious metal slowed.
"We're all aware of the current conditions in India, but India hasn't been a strong player in the market this
year," said Ani Markova, who manages $600 million at AGF Precious Metals Fund, with about 13% allocated
to gold and silver bullion.
"I think China is driving the bus this year," Ms. Markova said, adding that a pickup in China's exports has
helped offset some of the sluggish demand in India.
Still, India's dry spell is what is captivating gold investors now and likely to be the primary obstacle to higher
prices for the rest of the year, said Jon Nadler, senior metals analyst with Kitco Metals Inc. North America.
"Flat to negative returns in gold is entirely conceivable" for 2012 if the decline in India's demand accelerates,
Mr. Nadler said.
Rainfall in India as a whole is 20% below the 50-year average and, with the monsoon season half over, India
is facing the possibility of a full-blown drought in some regions. More than 60% of India's population directly
depend on agriculture.
While rains can pick up in August, the loss of water and lack of sowing triggered by a dry July are enough to
impair this season's agricultural output and could reduce the country's overall economic growth by about
0.6%, according to economists at UBS.
In India's northern state of Punjab, 27-year-old rice farmer Amardeep Singh lamented having to scrap plans
to throw his younger brother a wedding. Instead, he battled to save his newly sown crop by drawing out
groundwater with the help of a diesel-powered pump.
"All my savings are being used up in buying diesel. . . . The wedding plan will have to wait for at least a year,"
Mr. Singh said.

Investing in Funds: A Monthly Analysis --- Mixing It Up: Retirees Face Tough Choice on Pensions --- More employers offer workers the option...
Investing in Funds: A Monthly Analysis --- Mixing It Up: Retirees Face Tough Choice on Pensions ---
More employers offer workers the option to take a lump sum now rather than income for life
By Shefali Anand
1,024 words
6 August 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
R8
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
More and more retirees could soon be facing the choice of accepting a pension buyout or collecting a monthly
check, says Marilyn Capelli Dimitroff, a financial adviser in Bloomfield Hills, Mich.
Few companies still offer traditional, defined-benefit pension plans, but millions of retirees are still collecting
pensions. Earlier this year, General Motors Co. gave 42,000 of its 118,000 salaried retirees the option of
taking a one-time payment in lieu of a traditional pension, in a bid to reduce the company's overall pension
liabilities. That followed a similar offer by Ford Motor Co. to many of its former employees.
"This could well be the beginning of a trend for many companies," says Ms. Dimitroff, president of Capelli
Financial Services Inc.
In this column, we feature model portfolios and advice from prominent financial advisers who invest in mutual
funds and exchange-traded funds. Ms. Dimitroff has practiced as a financial planner since 1982. In 2009, she
was chairwoman of the board of the Certified Financial Planner Board of Standards, which administers the
CFP certification. Capelli Financial currently manages just short of $300 million in assets.
To make a decision on a pension buyout, Ms. Dimitroff says one has to look at factors including other sources
of income for the retiree and life expectancy.
A buyout may make sense if the retiree doesn't expect to live for very many years, she says. In that case, the
retiree may be leaving money on the table by taking a monthly check. By taking a one-time payment, the
retiree may even be able to leave something for heirs.
"If you take the lump sum, the big benefit that you have is flexibility," says Ms. Dimitroff. The money can be
put into investments that have the potential to grow faster than the inflation rate. In comparison, an
unchanging monthly check loses purchasing power over time.
Still, Ms. Dimitroff finds that for most people the monthly pension is the best option, mainly because it
provides some certain income. Ideally, she says, retirees have also accumulated a nest egg that can be
invested in stocks, bonds and other assets. "That can be used to mitigate the impact of inflation," she says.
Here, Ms. Dimitroff shares a model portfolio suitable for clients who can tolerate moderate risk. An actual
client would have twice as many funds. The weighted average return of the model portfolio, after fees, was
12.4% a year for the three years through June 30, according to Ms. Capelli. An actual client's return would
differ based on factors including additions and withdrawals of money.
Her firm charges a fee of 1% on the first $500,000 of assets invested and lower fees for additional assets.
There is also an initial financial-planning fee of $2,500 or more depending on the complexity of work involved,
and an annual planning fee starting at $600.
U.S. STOCKS: Long-term holdings form the core of the stock portfolio. The rest of the stock allocation
changes when the advisers see an opportunity for higher returns or reduced risk.
The advisers use momentum strategies, an approach in which investors typically buy what has been doing
well recently in the belief that it will continue to gain value.
Based on this, they made a few changes in the portfolio early last year. For one, they increased the
U.S.-stock allocation to 38%. Second, they added to large-company stocks while cutting back on small
stocks. Thirty percent of the overall portfolio is equally divided between two funds that buy large-company
shares -- Vanguard Growth ETF and Columbia Dividend Income.

--- Page 103 ---
Page 103 of 197 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The remainder of the U.S.-stock allocation, 8% of the portfolio, is in Columbia Acorn, which owns small and
medium-size companies.
FOREIGN STOCKS: The advisers have cut back on stocks in developed foreign countries since early last
year, to divert funds to U.S. stocks. The move was made "primarily because of the major concerns with
Europe," says Ms. Dimitroff.
They have added to their holdings in emerging markets.
Half of the portfolio's 10% foreign-stock allocation is in DFA Emerging Markets Core Equity. The rest is
divided between two funds that invest primarily in developed foreign countries: 3% in Harbor International,
which invests in large stocks, and 2% in Columbia Acorn International, which owns the shares of small and
medium-size companies.
BONDS: There is a 14% allocation to Pimco Total Return, which owns a wide variety of high-quality bonds.
"They are going to be the most aggressive in the bond portion of our portfolio," says Jason Close, a financial
adviser at Capelli Financial.
The portfolio has 13% in DFA Five-Year Global Fixed Income, which owns high-quality corporate and
government bonds from the U.S. and other developed countries.
To boost returns and income for clients, the advisers also own some funds that invest in lower-quality bonds.
They invest 7% of the model portfolio in Fidelity Floating Rate High Income.
The remaining 6% of the portfolio that is invested in bonds is in Treasury inflation-protected securities. For
some clients, the advisers own individual TIPS bought in 2009 and 2010. But for newer clients, they buy
Pimco 1-5 Year U.S. TIPS Index ETF.
ALTERNATIVES: Twelve percent of the portfolio is divided equally among three funds that invest in
nontraditional assets, including Cohen & Steers Realty Shares and the SPDR Gold Shares ETF. The third is
the JPMorgan Alerian MLP Index exchange-traded note, which basically promises returns that correspond to
an index of master limited partnerships that invest in energy facilities. "We look at it as exposure to
infrastructure," says Mr. Close. He also likes the ETN's yield, at more than 5% recently.

London 2012: The Weekend's Motto: 'Kiss Me, I'm British'
London 2012: The Weekend's Motto: 'Kiss Me, I'm British'
By Bruce Orwall, Matthew Futterman and Cassell Bryan-Low
776 words
6 August 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
B9
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
All that's left is for Jessica Ennis and Andy Murray to kiss on the balcony of Buckingham Palace.
The British, who rarely allow themselves to believe their sports dreams will come true, are suddenly awash in
wish fulfillment at their hometown Olympics. Their national squad, Team GB, spent the weekend executing
one fairy tale ending after another -- with some of its most popular athletes claiming eight gold medals on
Saturday and Sunday.
The result was an uncharacteristically exuberant weekend that left reserved Brits feeling as if they'd swapped
their Eeyore-like national sporting identity for some other country's.
British athletes won with their bikes, with their legs, with tennis rackets and oars and by doing flips and twists
over gymnastics equipment, sending Union Jacks waving through the streets and creating a spontaneous
catwalk of Team GB sportswear in the Olympic Park.
"It's our Games," said Terry Head, a retiree from West Sussex, wearing a t-shirt saying "British Bulldog" and
two small Union Jack flags tucked in his rucksack.
The team's Saturday night sweep in Olympic Stadium -- Ennis winning the heptathlon, Greg Rutherford the
long jump and Mo Farah the 10,000 meters and Greg Rutherford the long jump -- was the centerpiece. One
performance fed on another, with things getting so out of hand that Farah carried a stuffed Olympic mascot
on his victory lap and the crowd bellowed "God Save the Queen" through Ennis's medal ceremony.
"It was the greatest day of sport I have ever witnessed," said Sebastian Coe, chairman of London's
organizing committeea nd an Olympic middle-distance gold medalist himself. Saturday night was "like
glugging your way through a quart of cream, it just got better and better as the evening went on," he added
(to the befuddlement of the international sporting media).
Even as Sunday began with torrential rain, another of London's pre-Olympics worries, the party continued
unabated. Scottish tennis star Andy Murray, famously unable to win Wimbledon this summer (and every other
summer) finally won an Olympic gold there. Team GB also won gold in sailing.
Just last Wednesday, the nation was getting edgy when the Games' first five days came and went without any
gold. Then cycling hero Bradley Wiggins broke the spell with a gold medal in the team trials on Thursday,
loosening the floodgates.
News of the weekend's victories ping-ponged around London. Ennis's win at Olympic Stadium rocked the
bleachers miles away at the beach volleyball venue at Horse Guards Parade. Approaching 1 a.m. Sunday,
celebrants who may have been at the pub for awhile goaded their bicycle rickshaw drivers into an
Olympic-style race down Oxford Street. Firecrackers could be heard going off in placid St. John's Wood at
3:30 a.m.
Murray's Sunday win, an unthinkably easy stroll past Roger Federer, prompted an explosion of cheers on a
train headed to Olympic Park. I'm buzzing for Murray!" said Tony Hanks, a student on the train.
In the athlete's section of the gymnastics venue Sunday, British gymnast Kristian Thomas, who helped team
GB win a bronze medal last week, sat with the Dutchess of Cambridge, the former Kate Middleton, providing
private commentary. U.K. Prime Minister David Cameron spent some time Sunday tweeting niceties like
"@TeamGB is really flying." On Saturday, Paul McCartney, now properly addressed as Sir Paul, was spotted
at the Velodrome singing along to his own "Hey Jude" and cheering Britain's dominant track cyclists.
All this pent-up passion is understandable: It's been nearly half a century since England's last great sporting
coup on home soil -- the 1966 World Cup victory over West Germany. The lift also comes at an uncertain

--- Page 105 ---
Page 105 of 197 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
moment for the U.K., which is mired in a double-dip recession and, like much of the rest of Europe, making
severe public spending cuts. When the Olympics aren't in town, good times often seem a long way off.
Coe, who sat with Cameron Saturday night, said the British leader was "very seized by the need to build on
everything that he was seeing in the stadium, whether it was the extraordinary performances of our teams or
the ongoing challenges of getting more young people into sport."
But Coe, ever the pragmatist, also reminded Britons that the moment won't go on forever. "There is a limited
window of opportunity," he said.

But for a day, at least, Friday seemed to put one issue to rest: Given a choice between better-than-expected
economic data or more central-bank action, investors will take the better data. That sentiment ran counter to
some recent trends, when bad data have juiced the market-fueling expectations that governments will jump in
with actions that would boost shares. A strong jobs report would presumably make central-bank assistance
less likely.
Instead, after the strong jobs data, the S&P 500 erased everything it lost amid a four-session losing streak
and turned positive on the week.

expectations)," wrote Thomas Lee, chief equity strategist at J.P. Morgan Chase, in a research note to clients.

slid 75% over the previous two sessions after a software glitch caused it to make erratic trades in 148 stocks.
Knight has said the error, which has the broker struggling to stay afloat, likely will cost it $440 million.
European stocks rose, helped by the U.S. data, with the Stoxx Europe 600 up 2.4%. Spain's IBEX 35 index
jumped 6%.
Crude-oil prices rose 4.9%, to $91.40 a barrel. Gold prices added 1.2% to $1,606.00 a troy ounce. The dollar
fell against the euro but rose versus the yen.
In corporate news, Zipcar slid 3.88, or 37%, to 6.75 after the car-rental company trimmed its revenue outlook
for the year and said it brought in fewer new members than anticipated.

Turkey Trumps Syria Fears
World Stock Markets
Turkey Trumps Syria Fears
By Emre Peker
591 words
1 August 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
ISTANBUL -- Investors are flooding Turkey with cash even amid mounting risks the country may get mired in
the Syrian conflict, signaling that the demand for high returns from emerging markets is trumping fear in the
current environment.
Since June 1, investors poured about $12 billion into Turkey, doubling the assets purchased by foreigners
this year and triggering a rally in government debt that pushed yields on the benchmark two-year bonds down
as much as 1.72 percentage points to 7.75%, the lowest since September. The Istanbul Stock Exchange's
ISE 100 index since early June was up 17% to 65032.05 on Monday, the highest since May 2011, although it
fell slightly Tuesday to 64259.54.
In the past weeks, forces loyal to Syria's president have bombed opposition-held districts close to Turkey's
border, while Turkey's prime minister has threatened to take steps against a Kurdish group in Syria if it tries to
set up camps across the border. Syria in June downed a Turkish reconnaissance jet, and Turkey then
deployed troops along the border.
But rising risk appetite amid efforts by central banks in the developed world to support their economies has
investors poised to channel some of that cheap cash from the central banks to Turkey, fund managers and
analysts say. The country stands out among other emerging-market economies because data signal a
rebalancing of the economy, which is driven by surging exports but also underpinned by robust domestic
demand.
"There is a mitigating positive story that's been going on in Turkey; that's why people are tuning out the
external political risks," said Michael Cirami, who helps manage $13 billion of assets including
emerging-market debt at Eaton Vance in Boston.
In the past decade, Turkey's economy expanded an average 5.5% annually, while per capita income tripled
to more than $10,000 and exports surged to a record $135 billion in 2011.
Crafting a foreign policy of trade first, diplomacy later, Turkey recently has pursued economic interests more
aggressively than political power.
"The assumption is that Turkey's government, for all its rhetoric, will always act cautiously and pretty much
follow the line favored by its Western allies, said Robert O'Daly, a senior analyst at the Economist Intelligence
Unit in London. "And therefore it won't get sucked into the Syrian conflict."
Following last year's credit-fueled 8.5% economic expansion, Turkey is officially forecast to grow 4% in 2012,
while also reining in inflation to 8.9% in June from as high as 11.1% in April. The central bank expects
inflation to end the year at 6.2%.
Meanwhile, Turkish sales abroad rose 13% to $61 billion in the first five months of the year, driven by trade
with the Middle East and North Africa.
That diversification of markets supports Turkey's economic growth as activity stalls world-wide, along with its
strong domestic demand and the central bank's "cautious" stance backing the currency. Yet, a chance of
war looms as the Syrian conflict deepens.
"I can't say that these problems are fully priced" in, said Emir Tayman, head of sales at Istanbul-based
brokerage firm Ekspres Invest. "The tensions are affecting gold and oil prices, but not so much stock prices.
Plus, Turkey still has relatively high interest rates, as well as slowing inflation, which attracts investors. It's a
much bigger story than Syria alone."

The SmartMoney Report: As BlackRock's Rivals Chip Away, ETF Fees Could Be Next to Fall
The SmartMoney Report: As BlackRock's Rivals Chip Away, ETF Fees Could Be Next to Fall
By Ian Salisbury
476 words
31 July 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C5
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
BlackRock Inc., the world's largest provider of exchange-traded funds, is feeling hot under the collar -- and
that could be good news for investors.
In a July 6 earnings call with analysts, BlackRock Chief Executive Laurence Fink acknowledged that
Vanguard Group Inc.'s low-cost products were stealing his firm's customers. The admission caught some
experts by surprise, and since then shares of the firm have slid 6%.
"The incumbent doesn't usually talk about the upstart. It's an unwritten rule," says Todd Rosenbluth, an
analyst at S&P Capital IQ.
Of course, BlackRock remains the leader of the $1.2 trillion ETF market, with more than 270 funds covering
everything from Polish stocks to gold miners. The early lead of the company's iShares unit -- its oldest funds
date to the 1990s, long before ETFs went mainstream -- means many of its funds are the most-heavily traded
in their particular category. Such liquidity is prized by big investors because it makes buying and selling
shares cheaper.
But the firm has been undercut by low-cost competitors, most notably Vanguard, the mutual-fund giant that
has made a big push into ETFs in recent years. BlackRock's share of the ETF market has fallen to 40%, from
as high as 60% in 2006.
In the call, Mr. Fink promised changes without specifying what he had in mind. BlackRock declined to expand
on his remarks.
Although BlackRock has proved reluctant to cut fees, some experts say not doing so means risking its No. 1
title. Today, the average iShares stock fund levies fees totaling $46 per $10,000 invested, compared with $17
for Vanguard, according to S&P Capital IQ. "Cutting prices is the only thing you can do," says industry
consultant James Pacetti. "How else can you compete?"
One option, say some experts, is for the company to innovate once again. But with almost 1,500 ETFs now
available, analysts say coming up with something that still wows investors will be no easy task. BlackRock
already has a large stable of fund managers, so it could develop more ETFs built around active stock and
bond pickers rather than passive indexes. But although one active ETF, the $2.2 billion Pimco Total Return
ETF, has been a hit, most others have struck out.
One thing is clear, say industry observers: The pressure isn't likely to ease up. Last week Vanguard filed
paperwork to create a new ETF that will target short-duration Treasury inflation-protected securities,
essentially matching iShares Barclays 0-5 Year TIPS Bond Fund. Vanguard's proposed fee is $10 per
$10,000 invested -- precisely half the level charged by the iShares fund.

Markets Regain Footing but Not Their Faith
Abreast of the Market
Markets Regain Footing but Not Their Faith
By Tom Lauricella
1,029 words
30 July 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
This week marks an anniversary most investors would rather not celebrate. A year ago, markets entered the
most tumultuous period since the depths of the financial crisis.
As U.S. policy makers wrangled over the nation's debt ceiling, investors endured hair-raising plunges in stock
markets. Within seven days, the U.S. had lost its triple-A credit rating from Standard & Poor's.
There were dire predictions: Investors would flee U.S. Treasurys, sending the government's interest costs
higher. Stocks would plummet.
Looking back now, those bleak forecasts seem overblown. The flight of money from the U.S. never
materialized. Instead, investors flocked to Treasurys as a haven. Stock markets have recovered to new
postcrisis highs. On Friday, the Dow Jones Industrial Average closed above 13000 for the first time since May
7.
But one hallmark of that time remains: a loss of faith in decision makers that has permeated the financial
markets. From the U.S. to Europe, officials' failure to tackle their debt burdens has rocked investors'
confidence, driving many to the safety of Treasury bonds. While the Federal Reserve has helped underpin
the stock market by keeping interest rates near zero, many investors say they still have less money than
usual in stocks.
The result has been, and is likely to remain, increased volatility in stocks, sluggish growth and continued
rock-bottom interest rates, investors say.
This week brings several key events that will help shape investors' views. Meetings of the European Central
Bank and Fed will be followed by the release Friday of the July U.S. jobs report.
Investors still face a litany of worries. They range from Europe's unresolved debt crisis to the U.S. election to
the impending "fiscal cliff" at the end of the year, when lawmakers will have to decide whether to cancel
scheduled tax increases and spending cuts.
On the surface, it might not look like the debt-ceiling debacle and downgrade had any lasting impact. In the
U.S. Treasurys market, where some forecasts predicted a jump in interest rates, yields are significantly
lower.
The U.S. Treasury 10-year note is yielding 1.55%, down from 2.6% hours before the downgrade hit.
Despite a brutal selloff that came in August and September 2011, the Dow Jones Industrial Average is up
7.7% from Friday, July 29, right before the debt-ceiling deal was reached. The Dow is also up 14% from close
of trading on Friday, Aug. 5, just before S&P announced it had downgraded the U.S.'s long-term credit rating.
The price of gold is down slightly since then.
"On a stand-alone basis, the story is that the downgrade didn't matter," says Mohamed El-Erian, co-chief
investment officer at money manager Pacific Investment Management Co. in Newport Beach, Calif., a unit of
Allianz SE.
But the reluctance of leaders in the U.S. and Europe to find ways to address their debt burdens is weighing
on the sentiment of investors as well as corporate leaders, says Mr. El-Erian.
Many investors expect politicians to do the bare minimum needed to avoid a disaster for the economy. In
addition, some forecasters say the U.S. may again reach its debt ceiling in December, prompting a rerun of
last year's battle over whether or not to raise the limit.

--- Page 116 ---
Page 116 of 197 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Investors express little hope that the U.S. government's debt overhang will be meaningfully reduced, and they
see it as an impediment to economic growth. That pessimism feeds into expectations of continued frequent,
sharp swings in stocks and other riskier investments, while interest rates stay locked at extremely low levels,
some investors say.
Mr. El-Erian compares the country's debt overhang to a dark cloud "hanging outside your front door." While it
may not yet be raining, "you may not want to go out and it is perfectly rational for you to wait," he says.
Lon Burford, a financial adviser at Genovese Burford & Brothers, which is based in Sacramento, Calif. and
oversees $1.3 billion, says that is a big reason why he is keeping client stockholdings some 10 to 15
percentage points below the typical allocation for a portfolio. Making up the gap are greater-than-usual
holdings of alternative investments and bonds, with an emphasis on short-term and intermediate-term bonds,
Mr. Burford says.
"We think that last year's debt-ceiling fight was just an example of the dysfunctional political circumstances
not only in Washington, D.C., but globally," Mr. Burford says. "The drama that leads up to short-term deals
will continue to cause market volatility."
At Pimco, the view is that Washington and other capitals are too politically paralyzed to come up with
effective economic policies. Meanwhile, the Federal Reserve is becoming increasingly ineffective. The result,
Mr. El-Erian says, will be continued sluggish growth and low interest rates.
Last year's clash over the debt ceiling was a catalyst for Jason Trennert, founder of economic research firm
Strategas Research Partners, to abandon his two-year long bullish stance on stocks and turn bearish.
A year later, Mr. Trennert says, "Unfortunately, the federal government doesn't appear to have viewed it as a
wake-up call." That, he says, is largely because the downgrade didn't impose a lasting financial cost on the
U.S. government from investors demanding higher interest rates when buying the country's debt.
Along with slow growth and Fed policy, a key factor in sending rates lower was demand from global investors
who view the dollar a "reserve currency" in which they feel safe holding cash.
"Practically speaking, you learned that if you are the reserve currency you can get away with a lot," says Mr.
Trennert. "Unfortunately that is probably not a great lesson to learn," he says.
Early Asian Trading
Asian markets moved higher early Monday on hopes for more actions to deal with Europe's debt crisis.
Japan's Nikkei was up 0.9%, Australia's S&P ASX 200 was up 1.1% and South Korea's Kospi was up 1%.

World News -- Reporter's Journal: For Some Iranians, the Good Life Goes On --- Tehran Residents With Investments and Dollars Find Refuge...
World News -- Reporter's Journal: For Some Iranians, the Good Life Goes On --- Tehran Residents
With Investments and Dollars Find Refuge From the Country's Economic Woes
By Benoit Faucon
595 words
28 July 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A9
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
TEHRAN -- Iran may be facing its biggest economic challenge in years as international sanctions cut off its oil
revenue, but that hasn't made it any easier to find a table in an upscale restaurant in Tehran's leafy northern
suburbs.
For the wealthy here, life continues as normal. They enjoy social freedoms that would be unthinkable in the
more religiously conservative environments of neighboring Iraq or Afghanistan. Lady Gaga-style platform
shoes, loose veils with skull-and-crossbones designs and nose piercings are in evidence among young
women in Tehran's trendiest neighborhoods.
The richest segments of Iranian society -- including employees of the state and people with ample savings
and access to foreign currency -- are partly insulated from the inflation and cuts in state subsidies that have
afflicted most of Iran.
The government has increased pay for public servants by 15% to 20% since April, helping dent a much more
rapid increase in the price of daily staples such as bread and meat. Savings and bonds on government
ventures, such as natural-gas projects, are delivering returns of up to 25% to attract clients who face what is
now a 21% inflation rate.
A cushion of capital has put middle-class families in a better position to take advantage of opportunities that
arise as the economy weakens. The fall of the Iranian currency, which has declined as much as 70% since
December, has enabled some Iranians holding dollars or precious metals to make a small fortune.
In the Tehran bazaar, a noisy crowd gathered recently around a man shouting "currency exchange,
transfers." The money changer said he would charge a 20% commission to transfer $1,000 overseas. The
hefty fee was necessary, he said, because of the sudden and unpredictable moves in the exchange rate for
the rial. "The currency rates keep on changing," he said to justify the commission.
One chador-clad housewife from a wealthy suburb said she was making $1,000 a month by speculating on
gold. She buys gold coins at the bank and resells them in the bazaar to those who seek gold as a refuge
from the falling currency.
Others make money by charging a hefty premium on imported goods, which are becoming scarce as
countries refuse to ship directly to Iran.
One business consultant, speaking in a crowded North Tehran cafe, says the import trade -- with its 7%
markups -- is making the fortunes of a class of nouveau riche. "The Porsches, that's thanks to the 7%," he
says.
In another part of town, the Italian restaurant Boca -- charging $25 for dinner in a country with a typical
middle-class wage of $1,000 per month -- was turning away customers.
By contrast, poorer Iranians have little cushion from pay increases or extra cash to invest or speculate with.
In the south of the capital, Reza Obadi says he has been forced to work as a cleaner after losing his job in a
textile factory that closed down.
Iranians like Mr. Obadi have had to get used to sudden adjustments in their lifestyles. The rising price of
chicken, for example, has sparked what is being portrayed in some official media as a national crisis.
Mr. Obadi says he has had to forfeit chicken, and now lives on bread and cheese. "There is no social justice
in Iran," he laments.

Myths and Facts About the Gold Standard
Myths and Facts About the Gold Standard
By John H. Cochrane
1,168 words
28 July 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A17
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
While many people believe the United States should adopt a gold standard to guard against inflation or
deflation, and stabilize the economy, there are several reasons why this reform would not work. However,
there is a modern adaptation of the gold standard that could achieve a stable price level and avoid the many
disruptions brought upon the economy by monetary instability.
Let's start by clearing up some common misconceptions. Congressman Ron Paul's attraction to gold, and
Federal Reserve Chairman Ben Bernanke's biggest criticism, is that a gold standard implies an end to
monetary policy and the Federal Reserve. It does not.
Under a gold standard, the U.S. Treasury could exchange dollars for gold at a price of, say, $1,000 per
ounce. In practice, that means banks would freely exchange their dollar accounts at the Fed for electronic
claims to gold.
Nevertheless, the Fed could still buy government debt or other securities in exchange for newly created
reserves, lend its reserves to banks, and set interest rates on its loans to banks. A gold standard would not
stop the Fed from being the lender of last resort, bank regulator and financial crisis firehouse.
This isn't theory. It's history. The Bank of England operated an active monetary policy under a gold
standard for two and a half centuries. And the U.S. Federal Reserve was founded under the gold standard in
1914.
Moreover, the history of the gold standard is not just happy centuries of price-level stability. It is also a long
history of crises, devaluations, suspensions of convertibility, and defaults on sovereign debt.
Debauching the currency -- the great bugaboo of gold-standard champions -- will always remain a
temptation: If the government promises $1,000 per ounce and a recession comes along, it can say "we need
to stimulate. Now it's $1,100 per ounce." The success of a gold standard in achieving stable prices depends
heavily on its rules and commitments against devaluation -- rules honored in the past, until they weren't.
A gold standard does not eliminate debt crises or debt-induced inflation. No monetary system can absolve a
nation of its fiscal sins.
Imagine a government with $15 trillion of debt, $2 trillion of money outstanding, and $2 trillion of gold
reserves. Then its debt comes due. If the government can't raise tax revenues, cut spending, or persuade
investors to lend against credible future budget surpluses, it must print $15 trillion of cash not backed by gold,
devalue the currency, or default on the debt. Worse, if people see that outcome looming, they will run to
change their money for gold ahead of time, causing a crisis as the government's gold stocks run out.
A successful gold standard needs a clear way to deal with such crises. Here is one plan: Instead of printing
unbacked cash, the government lowers the coupon payments on its bonds and notes -- similar to the way
corporations can cut dividend payments. Of course, this is effectively a gentle "default" in times of stress. But
at least a fiscal impasse would not lead to a devaluation of the currency.
Yet if you don't expect magic, you are not disappointed by its absence. With these warnings, a modern
version of the gold standard is attractive.
Why not the old version? Most of all because the value of gold is poorly linked to other prices in the
economy, which is what we want to stabilize. Fixing the price of gold today would do little to control the
general price level. There are two big reasons for the disconnection between gold and other prices.
First, in the past, inventory demand for gold coins linked the value of gold to other goods. If prices rose,
people needed to hold more gold coins to make transactions. They would spend less on other goods and
services, which brought prices down again. But that channel is absent in a modern economy. Since people

--- Page 120 ---
Page 120 of 197 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
could buy and transfer gold deposits with a click of a mouse, nobody would have to hold substantial
inventories. And we are not going back to a 19th-century payments system based on lugging around gold
coins.
Second, features that made gold such good money in the past -- it is hard to produce and has few other uses
-- make its price especially badly connected to other prices. The relative price of gold has skyrocketed, yet
few of us abandon our jobs to go mine gold, and few of us substitute buying gold to buy other things. These
economic pressures to realign gold and other prices are nearly absent.
The solution is pretty simple. A gold standard is ultimately a commitment to exchange each dollar for
something real. An inflation-indexed bond also has a constant, real value. If the Consumer Price Index (CPI)
rises to 120 from 100, the bond pays 20% more, so your real purchasing power is protected. In place of gold,
the Fed or the Treasury could freely buy and sell such inflation-linked securities at fixed prices. This policy
would protect against deflation as well as inflation, automatically providing more money when there is a true
demand for it, as in the financial crisis.
The Fed currently interprets "price stability" to mean 2% inflation forever. A CPI standard could enforce 2%
inflation. But why not establish a price-level target instead? The CPI could be the same 30 years from now
as it is today, and long-term contracts could carry no inflation risk.
The Fed's main objection to a price-level target has been that 2% inflation gives it more stimulating power.
With 2% inflation, setting a nominal interest rate of zero allows the Fed to achieve a negative 2% real
interest rate, which may encourage people to borrow even more than at a zero real rate. Whether such
interest-rate stimulation is needed, wise, successful on average, and worth its cost of perpetual inflation is
the key question. I think not.
More deeply, the history of discretionary, shoot-from-the-hip monetary policy is one misstep after another,
and of turbulence induced by guessing what the Fed will do. Since the demise of the gold standard,
thoughtful economists have been searching for a replacement rule -- Milton Friedman's money-growth rule,
for example, John Taylor's interest-rate rule, and inflation or nominal GDP targets. Rules advocates
understand that the economy works better overall with stable units, rather than the government manipulating
units to trick us into buying more or less. A price-level standard is a firm rule.
In sum, a rule like the CPI standard could achieve the price-level stability that motivates the longing for a
return to gold.
---
Mr. Cochrane is a professor of finance at the University of Chicago Booth School of Business and a senior
fellow at Stanford University's Hoover Institution.

India Tries to Lure Back Individuals --- Government Hopes a Tax Break Will Attract Investors Disillusioned by Lower Returns, Volatility
World Stock Markets
India Tries to Lure Back Individuals --- Government Hopes a Tax Break Will Attract Investors
Disillusioned by Lower Returns, Volatility
By Ashutosh Joshi
798 words
26 July 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The Indian government's effort to attract individuals into the stock market faces a big hurdle: Investors are
increasingly disillusioned by investing.
The government plans to launch a program as early as next week to give a tax break to individuals for
investing as much as 50,000 rupees ($890) in stocks each year, according to a senior official in the ministry
of finance.
While that could be an incentive for individuals, it may not be enough to bring them flocking back. In recent
years, retail investors have been put off by the lower returns and extreme volatility of the market, say brokers
and financial advisers.
Of course, a sharp rebound in share prices could cause another rush into the market like the one between
2005 and 2007. But few observers see that soon, so the government's efforts to stimulate investing will likely
be met by a skeptical public for now.
Raja Banerji, a 39-year-old Gurgaon resident, invested in individual stocks for the first time around three
years ago when, he says, two brokerage firms promised him returns of 36% and 48% annually. He invested
more than 700,000 rupees. Now, his portfolio is worth 150,000 rupees.
"Even if a mad dog bites me, I'm definitely not going back into the stock market," says Mr. Banerji.
He attributes a large part of the loss to excessive trading, including in derivatives, by his brokers. He asked
his brokers to stop trading on his behalf but continues to own some stocks, hoping they will recover their
value over the long term.
India's benchmark 30-share Sensitive Index is up a total 10% over the past three years, which translates into
an annual return of 4% to 5% after including dividends. But that can't match the interest rate of 8% to 9% on a
typical bank deposit. Meanwhile, mutual funds that invest in gold have risen on average by 25% a year over
the past three years, according to Value Research.
India's government is tackling a phenomenon that has been echoed globally. In the U.S., individuals also
have shied away from stocks, withdrawing billions of dollars from stock mutual funds and curbing their
trading.
"Falling retail participation in equity markets is a matter of concern," said Thomas Mathew, joint secretary of
the capital markets division at India's Department of Economic Affairs. "The underlying aim of the scheme is
to develop equity culture in the country through which we can enhance depth and curb volatility."
He said he expects the program to be officially announced in the next couple of weeks. The deduction would
be available only to individuals with an annual income of less than one million rupees, and investors would
have to hold their stocks for at least three years.
The average daily cash trading volume -- driven largely by individual investors who actively trade in and out of
stocks -- on the National Stock Exchange in June was 96 billion rupees, down sharply from 219 billion rupees
three years earlier.
Flows into equity mutual funds, primarily owned by individual investors, have ebbed. In the first six months of
2012, investors withdrew around 35 billion rupees from these funds, versus a net investment of 68 billion
rupees in 2011.

--- Page 122 ---
Page 122 of 197 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Some financial advisers say investors are even stopping their periodic investments into funds, via systematic
investment plans.
"This is mainly because people's monthly installments for home, other loans have gone up," says Vishal
Dhawan, a financial planner in Mumbai. India's high lending rates and inflation put a damper on people's
savings.
Individuals are increasingly choosing other investment avenues, according to brokers and some individuals.
"Why should one invest in stock markets, when you get more than 8% tax-free returns from government
bonds?" said Chandrashekhar Patwardhan, a 49-year-old resident of Pune who has been an active investor
in stocks for more than 10 years. This year, he said he has made only a few individual stock bets. The bulk of
his investments has been in bonds, debentures and bank deposits, he says, because he believes the future
for stocks looks bleak.
"I don't think [the Sensex] will hit 20,000 level again anytime soon," he said. Wednesday, the Sensex closed
at 116846.05.
Brokers say it is important that India's stock market has more participation from individuals to help reduce
overall market volatility.
"This [tax break] is a useful tool for developing the investment culture in the country," says Dinesh Garg,
chairman at Delhi brokerage firm Quest Securities Ltd.

Stimulus Hopes Boost Gold
Commodities Report
Stimulus Hopes Boost Gold
By Matt Day
480 words
26 July 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Gold futures finished above $1,600 an ounce for the first time in more than two weeks as
some traders bet that a deteriorating global growth outlook would compel the Federal Reserve to try to
stimulate economic activity.
Wednesday's economic data provided fresh evidence that the world's economy continues to struggle. In the
U.K, second-quarter gross domestic product contracted by 0.7%, the most since 2009, and, in Germany, a
closely watched gauge of business confidence, the Ifo Index, fell to its lowest level since March 2010. In the
U.S., June sales of newly built homes hit a five-month low, falling 8.4% from May to a seasonally adjusted
annual rate of 350,000.
"It seems like growth everywhere is deteriorating right now," said Bob Haberkorn, senior commodities broker
with RJO Futures. "The gold market thinks you're probably going to see the Fed's hand forced."
Media reports this week pointed to the chance that sluggish U.S. growth may prompt the Fed to take action at
its policy-making meeting next week.
Monetary easing from the central bank -- essentially making more money available in order to lower lending
costs and spur growth -- tends to drive down the value of the U.S. dollar. Easing also can raise the prospect
of inflation down the line, stoking demand for gold as a hedge against rising prices.
The most-actively traded gold contract, for August delivery, rose $31.90, or 2%, to settle at $1,608.10 a troy
ounce on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange, the highest settlement price since July 5.
The front-month July contract rose $32, or 2%, to settle at $1,608 a troy ounce.
Gold futures surged earlier this year, nearing $1,800 an ounce on the view the Fed would implement
another round of easing. But the central bank disappointed gold bulls by largely holding to previously
disclosed policies. Meanwhile, investors' concerns about Europe's debt crisis pushed the dollar up sharply,
weighing on gold. The metal tends to move inversely to the dollar, as a falling dollar makes gold cheaper for
purchasers using other currencies.
Given that precious metals have struggled to draw buyers this year despite a full slate of economic worries,
stimulus hopes are "all that gold has going for it at this time," said Ira Epstein of the Linn Group.
Gold's gains Wednesday also came as investors shifted positions ahead of Thursday's expiration of August
options contracts, traders said. Ahead of the expiration of monthly options on Comex, gold can gravitate
toward round numbers, such as $1,600. Such marks correspond to the vast majority of the outstanding
contracts; thousands become worthless after Thursday if futures are below $1,600.

The Brits Rule World of Wheels
Sports
The Brits Rule World of Wheels
By Jason Gay
993 words
23 July 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
B8
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The Tour de France finale is a fashionable kind of chaos -- the race rushes into Paris, the colorful peloton
swarms the cobbles of the Champs-Elysees, madmen try to escape the pack by riding the smooth gutters by
the curbstone. This last stage gets derided as "ceremonial," since the race's overall winner is usually
determined a day or so before, but there's still a furious push to win it, as it's one more finish line, one last
chance for victory, and besides, you know, Paris. Come on. Like anyone needs a reason to get excited.
But there was an additional thrill on Sunday, in the closing seconds of the 99th Tour, to see, at the front of the
race, not just brawny sprinters and last-minute glory-seekers but the yellow jersey itself, on the spindly
shoulders of the race's leader, Bradley Wiggins of Britain.
Tour fans have grown accustomed to watching the yellow jersey take a careful spin into Paris, protected by
cautious teammates doing everything but feeding him Cheerios and showing Dora the Explorer DVDs, but
here was Wiggins, an Olympic medalist in track racing, surging to the front of the pack as the race bolted
down the Rue de Rivoli.
This was demanding, dicey work: Wiggins was trying to help his team, Sky, deliver its sprinter, Mark
Cavendish, to the finish line, and the sensation wasn't unlike watching Tom Brady rumble down the sideline
and throw repeated blocks for a fullback. That is, if Tom Brady did it in spandex at around 40 miles per hour.
The maneuver worked. Wiggins gave way to teammate Edvald Boassen Hagen, who yielded to the confident
dynamo Cavendish, who crushed the last meters, head low and bulging over his handlebars, looking like a
hound closing in on the bumper of a Formula One car. This was Cavendish's 23rd stage win at the Tour, and
his fourth consecutive closer on the Champs-Elysees, and if you are surprised he won, you are definitely not
Mark Cavendish.
Rolling in, seconds later, was Wiggins, safe and in yellow, the first British champion in the history of the Tour.
Could his sense of the moment be any sharper? A 32-year-old Londoner wins cycling's most prestigious race,
the week the Summer Olympics begin in the city where he grew up.
A surprise? Not really. The 2012 Tour was thought to be well-suited to Wiggins' strength as a time-trial rider,
and the absence of contenders like Alberto Contador (banned) and Andy Schleck (injured) opened up the
field. Wiggins had already won three stage races this season, and a fourth-place finish at the 2009 Tour
provided an undercurrent of belief.
But yellow would not be easy. It never is, when you consider the abundance of good fortune that any Tour
contender -- no matter how fit or accomplished -- needs to ride into Paris as the leader. Just the year before,
Wiggins crashed out of the Tour with a broken collarbone.
This year he still had to contend with the race's 2011 champion, Cadel Evans of Australia.
As it turned out, the rider closest to Wiggins proved to be his own colleague, Chris Froome of Team Sky. (The
Sky team is sponsored by British Sky Broadcasting Group PLC, in which Wall Street Journal parent News
Corp. owns a roughly 39% stake.) Second at the Vuelta a Espana last year, Froome began the Tour as one
of Wiggins's sentinels, but as the race entered the mountains, it became obvious that the Kenyan-born rider
possessed excellent climbing form. Was Froome being held back out of deference to Wiggins? Cycling fans
goosed the tension; Wiggins's wife and Froome's girlfriend traded jabs Twitter.
The awkwardness peaked in Thursday's final mountain stage when Froome repeatedly turned and held up for
Wiggins as another rider went on to win the stage.
Had this zesty intrasquad drama been playing out, on, say, the Miami Heat, ESPN would have gone into a
five-alarm frenzy. But cycling is a damaged sport still rich in etiquette, and it was clear that Sky expected the

--- Page 125 ---
Page 125 of 197 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
27-year-old Froome to wait his turn. Wiggins was magnanimous -- "He will win this race one day and I will be
there to support him," he said of Froome -- but on Saturday, as if fed up by the second-guessing, he went out
and scorched the Tour's last time trial, beating his teammate by more than one minute.
Definitive? Maybe. A statement? Absolutely. Froome wound up second, 23-year-old U.S. rider Tejay van
Garderen finished 5th, but the 2012 Tour belonged to Wiggins. And the ex-track star with scruffy mod
sideburns asked cycling fans -- burned so many times before by doping scandals -- to believe. Early in the
Tour, Wiggins raged about anonymous skeptics on Twitter, chiding them for "bone-idleness," but later in the
race he wrote a piece in The Guardian in which he described his cycling ascension and his rejection of
performance-enhancing drugs.
"If I doped I would potentially stand to lose everything," Wiggins wrote. "It's a long list. My reputation, my
livelihood, my marriage, my family, my house."
Now Wiggins moves on to London, a fresh sports icon in his country. He isn't a favorite in Saturday's Olympic
road race; once more, he is expected to assist Cavendish, who wants that victory so badly he might bite the
finish line in two. But there's also a time trial, and Wiggins is a contender there. These are thrilling days for
British cycling, on the rise for years now, and gold is always an appealing color. But nothing looks better than
yellow, and nobody knows this better than the man who wore it Sunday in Paris, right at the front.

Wednesday's Markets
Stocks Surge, In Black For July

NEW YORK -- Investors pushed stocks sharply higher, erasing all of July's losses, as investors reacted to
strong corporate earnings and continued hopes for central-bank intervention.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 103.16 points, or 0.81%, to 12908.70, while the Standard & Poor's
500-stock index tacked on 9.11 points, or 0.67%, to 1372.78 -- bringing that index to within a hair of a fresh

The Nasdaq Composite outpaced the other two measures as technology stocks soared, adding 32.56 points,

Intel led the gains among Dow components, rising 83 cents, or 3.3%, to $26.21 after the blue-chip

outlook, citing a more challenging macroeconomic environment.
Positive earnings news from VMWare and EMC also supported the sector. The top five gainers on the Dow
were all technology companies, led by Intel, Cisco Systems, Microsoft, International Business Machines and
Hewlett-Packard. Each of them rose 2% or more.
Industrial stocks were strong after Honeywell International reported second-quarter earnings that beat
estimates and raised the lower end of the range of its full-year earnings outlook.
Honeywell rose 3.64, or 6.7%, to 58.18. Fellow industrial giants United Technologies and Boeing also

"Wall Street has been breathing a sigh of relief, because even the companies that miss haven't missed by
much. So far, we're hanging in there," said Maris Ogg, president of Tower Bridge Advisors in Conshohocken,

Pulling on the downside were financial and consumer-staples stocks. Bank of America dragged on the Dow,
falling 39 cents, or 4.9%, to 7.53 after the Charlotte, N.C., lender's second-quarter earnings topped forecasts

European stocks gained after news that U.K. unemployment declined. London's FTSE 100 rose 1%, while the
