OFF DUTY --- Adventure & Travel: Going for the Gold --- Got a thing for nuggets? Head for the mountains of southern Alaska, and take home...
OFF DUTY --- Adventure & Travel: Going for the Gold --- Got a thing for nuggets? Head for the
mountains of southern Alaska, and take home any riches you find
By Bill Streever
1,437 words
16 June 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
D6
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
An ounce of gold brings about $1,600 these days. That doesn't impress the manager of Crow Creek Gold
Mine. "If I wanted to get rich," he tells me, "I wouldn't be running a gold mine."
Late in the 19th century, when Crow Creek was one of Alaska's richest mines, men built dams upstream and
piped water down, using the built-up pressure to blast away hills and banks in their hunt for precious metal.
They pumped and dug and sifted. They built long wooden sluice boxes in the bed of the creek. They erected
buildings -- a blacksmith shed, a bunk house, a cook house -- some of which still stand.
But they did not get all the gold. The miners missed some spots altogether. Gold sneaked through their
sluice boxes and returned to the creek bed.
The leftovers are part of what draws modern visitors to Crow Creek, an off-the-beaten-path attraction in
southern Alaska where anyone can crouch on a sand bar with a gold pan, surrounded by spruce and the
willow thickets that grow just below tree line. They can keep an eye out for bears while looking for gold using
tools and methods that would make perfect sense to the miners who flocked to Alaska when California's gold
fields of yore lost their luster.
The odds don't run too high in their favor. Visitors might imagine finding a multi-ounce lump of gold under a
rock or pulling a handful of nuggets out of a pan. In reality, in a full season, an experienced miner might pull
several pounds of gold from the creek, a load worth tens of thousands of dollars. Or he might find several
pennyweight, worth only hundreds. Beginners can expect to retrieve a few flakes to show friends back home.
But that's OK. People dig in part for the thrill of the chase, to feel their muscles work and to fool around with
mining equipment -- pans and shovels, but also specialized pumps and motors and hoses mounted on floats,
the toys of the trade.
For all of those reasons, I join two experienced amateurs for a day at the mine. Friends of a friend, they are
passionate about the search for gold and eager to share the experience. Gold fever has taken them beyond
panning; they have invested in their own suction dredge and sluice box.
Summer is the best time to wade into an Alaskan creek. I visit in October. Frost covers the ground, and in
places puddles have turned to ice. We zip ourselves into dry suits and walk into shadows cast by spruce
boughs. I grip a muddy, knotted rope and scamper backward down 75 feet of nearly vertical bluff into
knee-deep water. I wade 50 feet upstream through white water and strap on a diving mask and snorkel.
Then, face down in the current, I manhandle the business end of the suction dredge, digging a hole into the
streambed in search of the metal that made this waterway famous.
It's not the most glamorous pursuit I've ever undertaken. The dredge hose sucks up sand and gravel and
small rocks in a swirling vortex. I pick out and toss away the rocks big enough to clog the hose. Some are
two-handers. The biggest rocks require all of my strength.
Gold tends to settle into the bottom of the creek, resting on bedrock or on banks of clay. The idea is to move
away the surface rocks and sand to reach it. With my head underwater, I hear the gasoline motor that drives
the dredge over the noise of flowing water. I hear rocks banging against the steel end of my suction hose.
The hole is now three feet deep. I take a breath and submerge, nose against the streambed, hoping to see
yellow. Instead I see gray and black sand and a lot of rocks. The pay zone, if there is one, will be deeper.
"Gold is funny," one of the miners tells me. "There may be none here, but two feet over it could be thick."
By "thick," he means we may find a few pennyweight, a fraction of an ounce. The men I'm with have real jobs.
The occasional gold find fills their conversations, but so far not their pocketbooks.

--- Page 185 ---
Page 185 of 197 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
At this depth, the best we'll find is flood gold -- flakes or small nuggets that the miners call "pickers" because
they are just big enough to pick up. We have to go deeper. We take turns on the dredge.
Our diggings go through the hose and into the sluice box, a metal chute a foot across and four feet long, its
floor crossed by ridges. Gold and black sand, being heavier than almost everything else, get trapped
between the box's ridges or in the plastic carpet called "miner's moss" that lines the bottom of the box. The
lighter gravel and gray sand and small rocks flow out the tail end. The same principle works in gold pans,
with most material sloshing over the edge to leave behind heavier black sand and yellow bits, the stuff that
paves dreams.
Nature itself acts like a sluice box. Glaciers plow the mountains around us. Ice pulverizes granite and quartz
into sand and sends it downhill. As everything flows along the creek bed, the light stuff moves downstream
and the heavy sinks. The heaviest stuff sinks with the greatest resolve.
This is placer gold, not to be mistaken for the gold found in veins deep within the mountains. The veins that
fed this creek may no longer exist, having been mined by glaciers long ago.
With two hours of digging behind us, we shut down the dredge and squat around the sluice box, staring into
the dark sand. We find a fleck of gold, and then another, and ultimately five tiny pieces, the collective value of
which will not buy five minutes worth of gasoline.
But that's not the point. The point is the search itself. The point is that the next three minutes of dredging or
another rock moved might turn up a little pile of nuggets.
A pull on the starter cord kicks off the dredge. Glacial melt water bites my cheeks and my hands move rocks.
I look for color. Finding none, I am not disappointed. There is pleasure in simply being in the spruce forest,
face down in a creek, searching. And eventually there may be the thrill of finding something worth $1,600 per
ounce. If I were willing to sell it.
---
THE LOWDOWN: GIRDWOOD, ALASKA
Getting There: Girdwood, the town closest to the Historic Crow Creek Gold Mine, is about a one-hour drive
from Anchorage.
Looking for Gold: The Historic Crow Creek Gold Mine (www.crowcreekmine.com) is a 15-minute drive from
downtown Girdwood. Entry is $10. For $20, mine employees will show you how to use a pan. Sluice boxes
can also be rented for $20. After visiting a few times, you might be able to find a local miner willing to share
knowledge and dredging equipment in exchange for hard work.
When to Go: The mine is open to visitors from May 15 through Sept. 15. Private tours can be arranged at
other times of year.
Where to Stay: Girdwood's Alyeska Hotel (from about $200 per night, alyeskaresort.com) has around 300
rooms, and is popular among Alaskans and visitors. Girdwood also has several bed-and-breakfasts.
Campsites are available at Crow Creek Gold Mine for $10 a night (they don't take reservations).
Where to Eat: In Girdwood, the Double Musky Inn offers Alaskan seafood cooked New Orleans style
(doublemuskyinn.com). Chair Five Restaurant (chairfive.com) serves up beef and halibut burgers. It also
specifically prohibits "Gunfights, Fist Fights, Food Fights, or Cursing."
Other Things to Do: Girdwood is mainly known as a ski town, but offers plenty of outdoor activities in summer.
Take a tram up Mount Alyeska to check out the views of mountains and glaciers. Hike Crow Pass Trail, part
of the Iditarod National Historic Trail. You can also go kayaking, white-water rafting, fishing, cycling, glacier
cruising and more.
Remember: If you go to Girdwood to find gold, you may be disappointed. If you go there to search for gold,
you will be delighted. Either way, you are likely to leave with a flake or two.

REVIEW --- Ideas Market -- Week in Words: A field guide to unusual words in this week's Wall Street Journal
REVIEW --- Ideas Market -- Week in Words: A field guide to unusual words in this week's Wall Street
Journal
By Erin McKean
392 words
16 June 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4

Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
To Every Market, Turn, Turn, Turn . . .
Swirlogram
Goldman Sachs economists have plotted the momentum of their global leading indicator against what it says
about growth and come up with a graph they have dubbed the "Swirlogram." For the pessimists among you, it
looks like an accelerating downward spiral. . . .
-- "Overheard," Heard on the Street, Money & Investing, June 8
The suffix -gram comes from a Greek word meaning "something written" or "letter of the alphabet." Other
less-common -grams include the "phantogram" (a kind of optical illusion where a 2-D image appears 3-D from
certain angles) and "pyelogram" (an x-ray of the kidneys and bladder using radio-opaque dye).
---
Molasses Management
frozen middle
The famously slow GM bureaucracy -- that 1980s-era CEO Roger B. Smith dubbed the "frozen middle" --
lives on. Executives in Detroit say they face pushback from middle managers on decisions about everything
from engines to pensions to office furniture.
-- "GM's Chief Labors to Get Rebuilt Car Maker Into Gear," Page One, June 11
Another "middle" is the 2011 Oxford word of the year, "squeezed middle" ("the section of society regarded as
particularly affected by inflation, wage freezes and cuts in public spending").
---
So Colorful, It's a Crime
spraywork
An audacious (and illegal) piece of spraywork carried out on the exterior of a local subway carriage in May
2010 by two expatriates, for instance, went unreported for two days as the train ran its usual east-west route.
-- "Sticker Lady Spawns New Debate: Is 'Sanctioned' Art Cool?," Southeast Asia Real Time blog, June 14
Spraywork is a type of graffiti (the word graffiti comes from a Greek word meaning "to write").
---
A Pasha's Plumage
aigrette
A society newspaper reported that all eyes were on the beautiful young man, "dressed in the most
magnificent attire: his turban was surmounted by an aigrette and a crescent of diamonds; he wore a pale
green tunic covered in gold embroidery . . .."
-- "Orientalism Inverted," Leisure & Arts, June 14

The word aigrette comes from the French word for egret (the Lesser White Heron), because of the bird's
dramatic crested head.

Berlin's Ballooning Bailout Bill
Heard on the Street
Berlin's Ballooning Bailout Bill
By Richard Barley
516 words
13 June 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C12
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
[Financial Analysis and Commentary]
A billion here, a billion there, and soon you are talking real money. A true fiscal union may still be a distant
prospect. But each round of the European sovereign-debt crisis has seen Germany make financial
commitments that have raised the cost it would bear if the euro failed.
This week's Spanish bailout will raise its exposure to financially stressed countries by as much as 25 billion
euros ($31.2 billion) or so. Is Germany now in too deep to risk the single currency collapsing?
Germany's total commitment to crisis-fighting programs so far amounts to 113 billion euros, or 4.4% of
German GDP, Credit Suisse notes. This includes loans to Greece, European Financial Stability Facility
guarantees and capital to be committed to the European Stability Mechanism.
If the entire resources of the EFSF and ESM are eventually called upon, Germany's exposure to these
facilities would be 401 billion euros, the bank estimates. While EFSF guarantees are contingent liabilities,
ESM capital must be funded via sovereign borrowing, increasing Germany's debt and deficit.
But these direct exposures are only part of the story. Germany also is indirectly exposed to other euro-zone
countries via the European Central Bank. One way is via the ECB's 212 billion euro holdings of financially
stressed government bonds, of which Germany's exposure is 57 billion euros based on its share of ECB
capital.
Another way is via the ECB's Target2 payments system, which manages cross-border obligations between
national central banks. Target2 balances have ballooned since the start of the crisis as central banks have
effectively taken over from private markets in funding the current-account deficits of financially stressed
nations.
Under the gold standard, these obligations would have been settled by transfers of gold. But under Target2,
they are simply registered as accounting claims and liabilities. Germany's Target2 surplus was 644 billion
euros in April. Its share of the 660 billion euro Target2 liabilities of Greece, Ireland, Italy, Portugal and Spain
is 179 billion euros, Credit Suisse says.
Adding together all the potential German exposure gets to 671 billion euros, or one-quarter of German GDP --
and rising, Credit Suisse says. How much of this Germany might lose if Greece left the euro or the single
currency fell apart is impossible to say.
It is fair to assume a country leaving the euro would default on some or all of its bailout loans. In theory,
Target2 balances would remain as claims on national central banks even if a country left the euro. In practice,
default on this debt, too, can't be ruled out.
And if the euro were to collapse, Germany and the rest of the currency bloc would undoubtedly suffer a
severe recession, adding to the costs.
Logic suggests that, given the scale of these potential costs, they will never actually be realized: Germany
and other creditor countries will act to save the euro. The snag is that, in a crisis, logic sometimes flies out the
window.

Page 189 of 197 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
REVIEW --- Books: A Cure for What Ails Us --- It is not enough to encourage enterprise and limit regulation -- we need a fixed system of...
REVIEW --- Books: A Cure for What Ails Us --- It is not enough to encourage enterprise and limit
regulation -- we need a fixed system of value
By James Grant
2,275 words
9 June 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C5
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Why Capitalism?
By Allan H. Meltzer
Oxford, 154 pages, $21.95
A Capitalism for the People
By Luigi Zingales
Basic, 304 pages, $27.99
Bailouts, bear markets and joblessness will surely put capitalism on the November ballot. How do you like
your enterprise, Mr. and Ms. American Voter -- free or stifled or a little something in between?
The 2012 election promises a clear ideological divide. Mitt Romney may be no Ron Paul, but neither is there
any mistaking him for Barack Obama. The former private-equity titan will likely take his stand with a kind of
almost-free-enterprise -- the welfare state as we knew it before ObamaCare, so-called quantitative easing
and the enterprise-thwarting dread that someone in Washington is dreaming up even costlier experiments
than the ones that have already failed.
"Capitalism" is the epithet that 19th-century collectivists foisted on the economic system of private property
and the invisible hand. It's a name that wins no friends for the cause of enterprise. A socialist, supposedly,
cares for society. A capitalist ostensibly loves only his stocks and steel mills and strikebreakers. The PR
battle was almost lost at the naming.
Books upholding capitalism or denouncing it are as old as the printing press -- as to the denunciation, indeed,
it is much older. Aristotle and the church fathers condemned the acquisitive spirit. Voltaire and Adam Smith
praised it. America's original WASPs were of two minds. Hard work and enterprise were godly virtues, but the
virtuous man, by practicing them, could hardly help getting rich. Which is when the trouble started. "Religion
begot prosperity," lamented Cotton Mather, "and the daughter devoured the mother."
As time went by, statism devoured prosperity. Today the American economy sleepwalks. Ultralow interest
rates starve the savers and finance the speculators. Unanchored exchange rates fire up talk of "currency
wars." Where have we gone wrong, and what must we do to turn right?
Allan H. Meltzer and Luigi Zingales, free-market economics professors, have some ideas. Each urges more
scope for enterprise and less for government, though neither protests against the monetary arrangements
that give the government nearly unchecked and arbitrary financial power. "Two Cheers for Capitalism" was
the title of Irving Kristol's qualified endorsement of American enterprise published in 1978. Messrs. Meltzer
and Zingales seem prepared to award most of a third cheer -- providing we adopt their simple suggestions.
Mr. Zingales prefaces his sometimes wonky policy prescriptions with a personal plea. "I came here in 1988
from Italy because I was trying to escape a system that was fundamentally unfair," he writes. Statism,
Italian-style, was that system. "I would gently suggest," he addresses his American readers, "that you have
no idea what it's like to live in a country where there is virtually no meritocracy and competition is considered
a sin."
Well, maybe you do have an inkling, Mr. Zingales reconsiders, as he enumerates the ways in which Silvio
Berlusconi's Italy seems to have followed him across the Atlantic. He attacks the notion that some banks are
too big to fail, and he excoriates the individuals who exploit this cozy arrangement. He names, for instance,
Jim Johnson, a former CEO of Fannie Mae and still a director of Goldman Sachs, and Robert Rubin, the

--- Page 190 ---
Page 190 of 197 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
one-time Treasury secretary and famously inattentive director of Citigroup who admitted in 2007 that he had
had "no familiarity at all with CDOs," the mortgage securities that blew a hole in the bank that was paying him
$17 million a year. On and off Wall Street, Mr. Zingales contends, crony capitalism is displacing fair and free
competition.
What to do about it? A chaired professor at the University of Chicago's Booth School of Business, Mr.
Zingales is out to rebuild the American meritocracy. He wants to shame Washington lobbyists, to empower
the American health-care consumer and to make corporate directors truly accountable to the shareholders.
As for business schools, they "should stand up for what they think is the individual responsibility of a good
capitalist."
In regulation, Mr. Zingales demands simplicity. Let us have three focused federal regulatory agencies, not the
chaos of competing bureaus that now confuse the situation. One of these entities would stand guard against
inflation, another would protect the consumer and a third would keep the banks off the rocks by heading off
financial crises before they come to pass (this one will hire clairvoyants).
And then, he adds, let us hold the functionaries' feet to the fire. We can grade the price-stability agency by
watching inflation expectations (the Treasury's inflation-protected securities provide a handy guide). The
consumer-protection agency will succeed or fail according to the level of public trust in American financial
institutions (surveys will elicit that information). And we will know all we have to know about the competence
of the panic-prevention board by monitoring the cost of insuring the big lumbering banks against insolvency
(the market in credit-default swaps will sound the alarm).
"Why Capitalism?" asks Allan H. Meltzer, star professor at Carnegie Mellon University, and he sensibly
answers: because it works. Kristol regretted the absence of a capitalist moral compass. None, really, is to be
had, Mr. Meltzer says. He quotes Immanuel Kant: "Out of timber so crooked as that from which man is made,
nothing entirely straight can ever be carved."
For the distracted, part-time observer of our economic and financial affairs, Mr. Meltzer's slim volume may fill
the bill. In less than 150 pages of text, he takes on the welfare state, bank regulators, America's fiscal mess
and foreign aid. Generally speaking, the author believes that that government is best which governs least and
that the price mechanism allocates resources better than the White House does. But he made a better case
for the redeeming power of markets in the first fat volume of his two-volume chronicle of the Fed, "A History
of the Federal Reserve" (2003). The relevant section deals with a depression that miraculously cured itself.
Between January 1920 and August 1921, the unemployment rate in the United States jumped to 14% or so
from about 2% (as it was then inexactly measured); wholesale prices plunged by more than 40%; and
industrial production fell by 23%. The farm economy reeled, and there were waves of business failures -- in
Kansas City, the firm of Truman & Jacobson, a men's-wear retailer, went bankrupt, though the first named
partner would recover his courage and later win the presidency. "Ain't we got fun?" is the mordant rhetorical
question posed by the title of the hit tune of 1921.
The administration of Warren G. Harding responded to this macroeconomic disaster by running a budgetary
surplus. The Fed didn't lower interest rates but raised them. In response to this bitter medicine, or perhaps
despite it, the economy staged the kind of bounce-back that the Obama administration can only pine for. In
1922, the first full year of recovery, industrial production leapt by 27.3%. By 1923, joblessness was back to
3%.
Mr. Meltzer, a monetarist, takes due note of the fact that, from the peak to the trough of the 1920-21 business
cycle, the sum of checking accounts and currency fell by 10.9%. Such a collapse, nowadays, would call forth
a gust of Federal Reserve intervention. Absent radical money printing -- "QE" was not even a gleam in the
central bank's eye at this point -- how did the American economy right itself? How did the banking system
survive?
One part of the answer, Mr. Meltzer says, was that gold rushed into the country. Because gold was money
(governments acknowledged it as such), the inflow delivered a monetary pick-me-up. There was no mystery
why funds moved to these shores: America was on the bargain counter. Stocks, bonds and commodities had
taken a beating. Value-minded foreigners seized the opportunity to buy them. No central banker had to lead
investors by the hand.
The plunge in prices meant that the dollar bills in American wallets went further, too. Mr. Meltzer calls this rise
in the purchasing power of money the "real balances" effect. "The public used its increase in money balances
to purchase goods and assets," he writes. "Judging from stock market prices, after July 1921 asset prices
rose absolutely and relative to prices of new production, stimulating the demand for new production. The
change in relative prices and real wealth more than offset the negative effect of high real interest rates on
spending." As for American banking, the biggest casualty was the little First National Bank of Cleburne,

--- Page 191 ---
Page 191 of 197 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Texas, with deposits of $2.8 million. No bank was designated "too big to fail" in those days -- and not one big
bank did.
For all the talking that Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke does about the Great Depression of the 1930s, he has
nothing to say about the not-great depression of the 1920s. It was ugly and sharp, but it ended 18 months
after it began. And in the course of it ending, the Treasury reduced the public debt to $22.9 billion from $24.3
billion. According to 21st-century doctrine, producers and consumers are incapable of climbing out of a
deflationary hole without a government-provided fiscal and monetary ladder. Nonetheless, in this particular
unsung depression, individuals managed the trick, which suggests that markets work if only we let them.
"In 1920-21," relates Benjamin Anderson, a bank economist who witnessed the goings-on, "we took our
losses, we readjusted our financial structure, we endured our depression, and in Aug. 1921 we started up
again." At any rate, the Harding depression presents a provocative comparison to the Hoover-and-Roosevelt
depression that began in 1929 and didn't end, as Robert Higgs persuasively argues in his 2006 book,
"Depression, War and Cold War," until 1946.
Neither Mr. Meltzer nor Mr. Zingales is a fan of the Federal Reserve -- Mr. Meltzer is an especially withering
critic -- but neither makes a fundamental case against the pure paper dollar, the Fed's stock in trade. Mr.
Meltzer declares that the principal alternative to fiat money, the gold standard, has no place in a modern
economy "because democratic governments, reflecting voters' concerns, prefer now to keep unemployment
rates low rather than stabilize prices via the price of gold." The alleged trade-off between stable prices and
unemployment under a gold standard, however, he asserts but does not prove. Nor does he pause to note
that, under the Bernanke standard, America's unemployment rate has topped 8% for 3 1/2 years running. For
no potential employer is uncertainty as to the timing of the next adventure in money printing a
confidence-builder.
Mr. Meltzer, a devotee of Milton Friedman, one of the 20th century's pre-eminent apologists for fiat money, is
true to the monetarist faith. For Mr. Zingales, however, I have high hopes -- perhaps in his next book. As he
detests statism, so may he come to recoil from modern monetary methods.
What are these methods? Fixing an interest rate, manipulating the structure of interest rates and goosing the
stock market in the name of "stimulating" the economy are prime examples. Many argue that these radical
interventions have spared us from another Great Depression. More likely, I think, the same machinations
have snuffed out whatever chance we had of dealing with our difficulties as efficiently as our forebears did in
the not-great depression of the early 1920s.
Not since 1971 has anyone had the right to exchange a dollar bill at the Treasury for a fixed and statutory
weight of gold or silver. Most of us have forgotten that the dollar was ever exchangeable into anything except
small coins. We collectively nod in agreement at the dubious proposition that the Fed should have the power
to manipulate the value of the money we spend and save, just as it suits the government's purposes.
Herbert Hoover is not often invoked as an authority on monetary economics, but the memoirs of the 31st
president are full of wise words on the relationship between the citizen and his government. The
gold-exchange standard of the late 1920s and 1930s, a third-rate version of the classical gold standard in
place before World War I, failed as a monetary mechanism. But it did serve a constitutional purpose. Hoover
wrote:
Currency convertible into gold of the legal specifications is a vital protection against economic manipulation
by the government. As long as currencies are convertible, governments cannot easily tamper with the price of
goods, and therefore the wage standards of the country. They cannot easily confiscate the savings of the
people by manipulation of inflation and deflation. . . . Once free of convertible standards, the executives of
every "managed-currency" country had gone on a spree of government spending, and the people thereby lost
control of the public purse -- their first defense against tyranny.
Mr. Meltzer and Mr. Zingales -- and Mr. Romney, too -- pledge their fealty to the ideal of the free market. But
each accepts a monetary system that replaces a fixed and objective standard of value with a changeable and
subjective one. Do you wonder how to restore monetary power in the people and put a spring back in the step
of the capitalists? "The True Gold Standard," by Lewis E. Lehrman, published in 2011, is available on
Amazon.
---
Mr. Grant is the editor of Grant's Interest Rate Observer.

Blue Chips Limp to a Win --- Stocks Jumped Early on China's Rate Cut, Then Stumbled on Fed's Inaction
Thursday's Markets
Blue Chips Limp to a Win --- Stocks Jumped Early on China's Rate Cut, Then Stumbled on Fed's
Inaction
By Matt Phillips
670 words
8 June 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
After springing from the gates, stocks struggled to the finish line Thursday after Federal Reserve Chairman
Ben Bernanke declined to commit to a fresh push to support the economy.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average was up by as much as 1.1% early in the day as investors reacted to
China's decision to cut interest rates, but it closed up 46.17 points, or 0.37%, to 12460.96, stumbling to its
third consecutive daily gain.
Thursday's gain marked the first time since late April that the Dow has been able to put together a winning
streak that long.
The blue chips are up roughly 3% from their recent bottom on Monday, but the surge of worry stemming from
the European crisis has taken a toll. The Dow is still down 5.7% since the end of April.
While it seemed to be losing steam Thursday, the recent rally has been driven by hopes for effective action
from policy makers tasked with challenges on a number of fronts: the continuing European crisis, the slowing
Chinese economy and a U.S. job market that remains in the doldrums.
"Markets aren't going up because they see some sort of robust expansion in the global economy," said
Michael Farr, president and chief investment officer at portfolio-management firm Farr, Miller & Washington,
which has about $800 million in assets under management.
"They see a robust expansion in potential stimulus from central banks," added Mr. Farr.
Shares of global industrial companies with strong ties to emerging markets helped pushed stocks higher, as
investors digested news that China's central bank cut interest rates in an effort to give the world's
second-largest economy a boost.
The decision by the People's Bank of China injected life into stocks of companies such as United
Technologies Corp., which gained 2.4% Thursday. In late April, the company had reported that new Chinese
orders at its Otis Elevator unit fell. Chicago-based aerospace giant Boeing Co., which also is positioning itself
for strong China-driven growth, rose 1.4%.
Still, China's rate cut also underscored that the Chinese economy, which has been a huge driver of global
growth, has started to sputter. The slowdown could eventually become a larger worry for investors.
"Everybody's happy to see this monetary easing take place," said Michael Shaoul, chairman of Marketfield
Asset Management in New York. "But I think what that has done is crystallize some of the fears about the
Chinese economy."
Meanwhile, solid news on the U.S. domestic economy also helped power a strong start for stocks.
The Labor Department said the number of U.S. workers filing initial applications for unemployment benefits
fell for the first time in more than a month.
But a late-morning appearance by the Fed's Mr. Bernanke before Congress's Joint Economic Committee
wrung some enthusiasm out of stocks.
The chairman listed several risks to the U.S. economic recovery but stopped short of signaling that Fed
action was imminent.

--- Page 193 ---
Page 193 of 197 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Early gains tapered off after his comments, as investors seemed to ratchet down their expectations about
another round of stimulus by the central bank.
Other markets also indicated that investors were rethinking the likelihood of another dose of easy-money
policy from the central bank.
Gold prices declined sharply on Mr. Bernanke's comments. Prices for gold, which some investors have
bought in recent years as a way to hedge against the chance that Fed action could eventually stoke inflation,
had risen in recent days along with expectations of a fresh round of Fed measures.
Gold futures lost $46.20 a troy ounce, or 2.83%, to close at $1586.60.
In Asia early Friday, shares were down. Japan's Nikkei was down 1.3%, Australia's S&P ASX 200 dropped
0.5% and South Korea's Kospi was 0.2% lower.

Stocks Shake Off Blues --- New Hopes that Central Banks Will Intervene Push Dow to Biggest Gain of Year
Wednesday's Markets
Stocks Shake Off Blues --- New Hopes that Central Banks Will Intervene Push Dow to Biggest Gain of
Year
By Jonathan Cheng and Tom Lauricella
735 words
7 June 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
For at least a day, investors embraced the possibility that central banks will smooth over the ills in Europe
and the U.S., pushing U.S. stocks to their biggest gains this year.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average jumped 286.84 points, or 2.37%, to 12414.79 Wednesday, its largest rise
since mid-December. The jump marked the first time since late April the index posted two consecutive days
of gains. Prior to the recent rebound, the Dow had slumped nearly 9% since May 1.
The advance continued early Thursday in Asia. In Japan, the Nikkei index was up 1.2%, while in Australia,
the S&P/ASX 200 was up 1.2%. South Korea's Kospi index was up 2.4%.
Rallying along with stocks were other investments that had suffered big price declines in recent months.
Crude-oil futures rose about 1% to about $85.02 a barrel and copper jumped 2.8%.
Stocks around the world have tumbled amid rising anxiety about whether European officials will be able to
avoid a disorderly exit from the euro zone by Greece, and possibly Spain. In the U.S., evidence is growing
that the economy is weakening, highlighted by a weak jobs report last Friday.
But on Wednesday, there were signs that officials on both sides of the Atlantic were beginning to mobilize in
an effort to battle slowing economic growth and tackle worsening problems in the euro zone, particularly
Spain.
"We were getting to the point where the bad news was going to become good news as it forced the hands of
policy makers," said Doug Sandler, chief equity officer at RiverFront Investment Group in Richmond, Va.
In the U.S. on Wednesday, Atlanta Fed President Dennis Lockhart said that "further monetary actions to
support the recovery will certainly need to be considered" if modest domestic growth is no longer realistic.
Those remarks came after The Wall Street Journal reported that Fed officials this month might discuss
whether more action is needed to spur the recovery.
Going into the final minutes of stock trading, share prices got another boost as San Francisco Fed President
John Williams said the Fed's easy money policy was "crucial."
"Investors are comforted by what seems like a Federal Reserve security blanket," said Jack Ablin, chief
investment officer at Chicago-based Harris Private Bank, which oversees about $60 billion in assets.
Rising expectations that the Fed will pump more money into the financial system through additional
purchases of long-term bonds, such as mortgage-backed securities, helped fuel a 1.1% increase in gold
prices Wednesday.
Meanwhile, investments that had been heavily sought out as safe havens, most notably U.S. Treasurys, were
hit by selling. The benchmark 10-year note's yield, which moves inversely to its price, posted the biggest
three-day increase in nearly three months to 1.651%.
"As bizarre and opposite as it sounds, the worse the situation gets, the more faith the markets will have in the
Fed coming to the rescue," said Robert Pavlik, chief market strategist at Banyan Partners.
At the same time, sentiment was given a boost by news suggesting European officials were moving closer to
a support package for Spanish banks.
And while the European Central Bank did not deliver a rate cut at its meeting Wednesday, expectations are
rising for a rate cut at the next ECB meeting in July.

--- Page 197 ---
Page 197 of 197 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Fueling that belief were comments from ECB president Mario Draghi, who said that "a few" members of the
ECB's rate-setting committee were in favor of a rate cut.
Stocks rose across the region and the euro rose 1% to $1.2582.
However, optimism for an ECB rate cut was tempered by signs that the ECB isn't prepared to take more
aggressive steps to combat the impact of the euro-zone debt crisis, such as ramp up open-market purchases
of European government bonds.
While there was plenty of news for traders to seize on Wednesday as a reason to bid up prices of stocks and
other riskier investments, the scope of the gains in stocks likely reflected the degree to which many in the
market had become negative in recent weeks.
---
Matt Jarzemsky and Steven Russolillo contributed to this article.
